Interpreting Ambiguous Numerical Acknowledgments
Rulings on interpreting ambiguous numerical expressions in acknowledgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules to interpret ambiguous numerical acknowledgments, distinguishing between weighed/measured items and items of value.

Core Idea
When an obscure number is followed by a specific item, the item explains the number; conjunctions and item type matter.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rules for interpreting ambiguous numbers in acknowledgments. If someone says 'one hundred and a dirham,' the dirham explains the hundred, making it 101 dirhams. But if he says 'one hundred and a garment,' the hundred is dirhams and the garment is separate. These detailed rules prevent disputes and ensure clarity in financial obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the construction
The acknowledgment contains an obscure number (e.g., mi'a – one hundred) and a conjoined item with 'wa' (and). The interpretation depends on the type of item.
The number is considered obscure (mubham) because it is unspecified what it refers to.
2
Classify the conjoined item
If the item is weighed (mawzūn) or measured (makīl), it becomes an explanation (bayān) of the number. If it is a single item of value (qīmī), it does not explain the number but is treated as an additional separate debt.
Weighed items: dirhams, dinars. Measured items: grains, liquids. Value items: garments, animals.
3
Apply to simple examples
'One hundred and a dirham' → 101 dirhams. 'One hundred and a garment' → 100 dirhams plus one garment. The difference is due to the item type.
The conjunction 'wa' in the first case merges; in the second, it adds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقر بدين مؤجل وادعى المقر له أنه حال لزمه حالاً كإقراره بعبد في يده أنه لرجل وأنه استأجره منه (ويستحلف المقر له فيهما) بخلاف ما لو أقر بالدراهم السود فكذبه في صفتها يلزمه أما أقر به فقط كإقرار الكفيل بدين مؤجل ولمن عليه دين مؤجل إذا خاف لو اعترف به لا يصدقه إنكار أصل الدين إذا لم يرد توى حقه ومن أقر بعدد مبهم وعطف موزوناً أو مكيلاً كان بياناً له (كمائة ودرهم) أو درهمان أو ثلاثة دراهم فهي دراهم) عطف عليه قيمياً واحداً (لا كمائة وثوب) أو وثوبان وإن متعدداً فبيان (كمائة وثلاثة أثواب ولو قال نصف درهم ودينار وثوب فعليه نصف كل أحد عشر ألفاً فيلزم أن تهدر الواو التي تعتبر مهما أمكن وهنا ممكن فيقال أحد وعشرون ألفاً ومائة وأحد وعشرون درهماً. نعم قوله ولو سدس «الخ) مستقيم أي يقال فيه مائة ألف وأحد وعشرون ألفاً ومائة وأحد وعشرون درهماً لا أنه مستقيم زيادة على العشرة آلاف مائة وثلاثة أثواب
And if he acknowledges a deferred debt and the claimant claims it is due immediately, it becomes due immediately – like his acknowledgment of a slave in his possession that he belongs to a man and that he hired him from him. And the claimant is made to take an oath in both cases. Unlike if he acknowledges black dirhams and then contradicts their description, he is liable. As for acknowledging only that – like the acknowledgment of a guarantor of a deferred debt, and for one upon whom a deferred debt is owed, if he fears that if he admits it, he will not be believed regarding denial of the original debt if he does not want to lose his right. And whoever acknowledges an obscure number and then conjoins a weighed or measured item, it becomes an explanation for it – (like: 'one hundred and a dirham' or 'two dirhams' or 'three dirhams,' they are dirhams) conjoined with one item of value (not like 'one hundred and a garment' or 'two garments,' and if multiple, it is explanation (like 'one hundred and three garments'). And if he says 'half a dirham, a dinar, and a garment,' he is liable for half of each. Eleven thousand: it necessitates dropping the conjunctive 'waw' which is considered whenever possible, and here it is possible, so it is said 'twenty-one thousand and one hundred and twenty-one dirhams.' Yes, his saying 'and a sixth' etc. is straightforward, i.e., it is said 'one hundred thousand and twenty-one thousand and one hundred and twenty-one dirhams,' not that it is straightforward as an increase over ten thousand. One hundred and three garments.

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: first, the ruling on acknowledging a deferred debt – if the creditor claims it is due immediately, the debtor must pay immediately, and the creditor takes an oath. An example is given of acknowledging a slave in one's possession belongs to another and that he hired him from him. Second, the passage deals with ambiguous numerical acknowledgments. When someone acknowledges a sum and then mentions a specific item, the item explains the number. For example, 'one hundred and a dirham' means the total is 101 dirhams (the dirham is added). But 'one hundred and a garment' means the hundred is dirhams and the garment is separate. If multiple items are mentioned, they explain the total. The passage also discusses complex constructions like 'half a dirham, a dinar, and a garment' – he owes half of each. The text includes an example of 'eleven thousand' which requires dropping the 'and' to avoid contradiction, etc.

Key Terms
عدد مبهم (adad mubham): An obscure number that does not specify what it is counting.
موزون (mawzūn): Weighed item, such as dirhams, dinars, or grains.
مكيل (makīl): Measured item, such as grains or liquids.
قيمي (qīmī): An item of value, such as a garment or animal, which is not typically weighed or measured for sale.

B) 103 garments
C) 100 garments plus 3 dirhams
D) 103 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ambiguous numerical acknowledgments are interpreted based on the type and number of conjoined items: weighed/measured items explain the number, single value items are separate, and multiple items of any type explain the number.

Acknowledging Items in Containers and Partial Property in Hanafi Law
Rulings on whether acknowledging an item in a container includes the container, and on partial acknowledgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an acknowledgment of an item in a container includes the container, and identify the exceptions based on custom.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, an acknowledgment in a container includes the container unless custom excludes it, as with a stable or house.

The Hanafi school provides detailed rules to determine what is included when someone acknowledges an item that is in a container or part of a larger property. These rules rely heavily on customary usage and the nature of the items. This lesson will guide you through the key rulings and their conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledged item and its container
Determine the specific item mentioned and the container or property it is associated with.
E.g., 'dates in a basket' or 'beast in a stable'.
2
Check if the container is customarily included
In Hanafi law, the container is included unless custom dictates otherwise. Stable and house are not included. Basket and saddle are included.
Custom is the basis; some containers are like parts of the thing.
3
Apply the specific examples from the passage
Study the examples: ring (band+stone), sword (blade+scabbard+baldric), bridal chamber (wood+covering). For 'garment in ten' only garment, etc.
Each example illustrates the rule or its exception.
4
Handle partial acknowledgments
Acknowledgment of 'half of this slave' is valid, but 'half of this dinar' is not because a dinar is indivisible in kind.
Partial acknowledgment is valid for divisible property.
5
Apply the ruling for acknowledgment to a fetus
Acknowledgment for a fetus is valid only if a valid cause (like bequest) is mentioned and its existence at that time is possible.
If stillborn, the property goes back to the heirs.

If he acknowledges dates in a basket, both are binding. For a beast in a stable, only the beast is binding. For a ring, the band and stone are included. For a sword, the blade, scabbard, and baldric are included. For a bridal Chamber, the wood and covering are included. For a garment in a handkerchief or in another garment, both are binding. For a garment in ten, he has the garment. For five in five – if he meant the beating, five and ten? If he meant with: for someone who has from one dirham to ten or between one dirham to ten, he has nine. For 'I have from my house between this wall and that wall,' he has only what is between them. The acknowledgment of a pregnancy is valid. And for the fetus, if he mentions a valid cause, it is valid; otherwise, no. And if he acknowledges with a condition of option, the property is binding and the condition is void. Likewise, half of this slave and this slave girl – unlike half of this dinar. And 'dirham' – a full dirham. And ten dirhams and a daniq and a qirat of silver. And if he acknowledges dates in a basket, or food in a sack, or a ship, or a garment in a handkerchief or a garment, the container is binding like the contents. But from a basket, not like a beast in a stable and a garment in ten and food in a house. And for a ring, he has the band and stone; for a sword, the blade, scabbard, and baldric; for a bridal chamber, the wood and covering; for five in five – if he meant the beating, five and ten? If he meant with: from one dirham to ten or between nine and a kor of wheat to a kor of barley, both are binding except a qafiz of barley. And ten dirhams to ten dinars, both are binding except a dinar. He has from my house between this wall and that wall: he has only what is between them. And the acknowledgment of a pregnancy – it is valid if its existence is possible at that time, even if non-human absolutely – unlike the acknowledgment for a suckling infant: it is valid even if he mentions an invalid cause like lending. And it is valid for the fetus if he mentions a valid cause; otherwise not, as if he is vague or mentions an invalid cause like a loan. It is only valid for the fetus if its existence at that time is known or probable, such that it could be born in less than its term if married, or less than two years from the time of separation if divorced. Then if she gives birth alive, the fetus has what was acknowledged for it; if stillborn, it reverts to the heirs of the testator or his father. If she gives birth to two, if both are male or female, it is between them halves; otherwise, similarly in will, and in inheritance, the male gets double the female.

Commentary
This passage details several specific rulings about acknowledgments involving containers and property. If one acknowledges 'dates in a basket,' both the dates and basket are included (the container is part of the acknowledgment). But if one acknowledges 'a beast in a stable,' only the beast is included, not the stable. For a ring, the band and stone; for a sword, the blade, scabbard, and baldric; for a bridal chamber, the wood and covering. If one acknowledges 'a garment in a handkerchief' or 'in another garment,' both are included. But 'a garment in ten' means only the garment, with ten as a description? The passage also discusses numerical ranges and partial acknowledgments like 'half of this slave and this slave girl' (valid) unlike 'half of this dinar' (not valid because it's not divisible in kind). Then it covers acknowledgment of pregnancy (for the fetus): it is valid if a valid cause is given (e.g., a bequest) and if the existence of the fetus at that time is possible. If stillborn, the acknowledged property goes back to the heirs of the testator or father. For twins, shares are equal if same gender; otherwise male gets double only in inheritance, not in will.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): A fetus; acknowledgment for a fetus is valid in Hanafi law if a valid cause (like bequest) is mentioned and the fetus's existence at that time is possible.

A) Only the band.
B) Only the stone.
C) Both the band and stone.
D) Nothing is included unless specified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the scope of an acknowledgment depends on custom and the nature of the items; containers are included unless custom excludes them, and partial acknowledgments are valid for divisible items only.

The Invalidity of Conditional Options in Acknowledgment
Ruling on conditional acknowledgment with commentary on terms and textual order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stipulating an option (khiyar) in an acknowledgment (iqrar) and identify the exceptions for sale and suretyship.

Core Idea
Stipulating an option in acknowledgment is void; the acknowledgment is binding immediately unless it is a sale or suretyship.

Acknowledgment (iqrar) is a legal declaration that creates a binding obligation. The Hanafi school strictly limits the use of contractual options (khiyar) in iqrar, treating any condition of option as void unless the acknowledgment itself is part of a valid contract that permits options. This lesson examines the default rule and its exceptions, clarifying when an option can survive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage addresses an acknowledgment (iqrar) where the acknowledger stipulates a condition that he has an option (khiyar). Identify the default hukm: the acknowledgment is binding, and the condition is void.
The condition is not automatically invalid; it is overridden by the binding nature of iqrar.
2
Note the exception for sale
If the acknowledgment is phrased as a sale contract (e.g., 'I sold you this for that'), and the seller stipulates an option, the option is valid unless the buyer denies the sale. This is because sale contracts can legally include options.
The acknowledgee (buyer) must not deny the contract; otherwise the option falls.
3
Note the exception for suretyship
An acknowledgment of a debt due to suretyship (kafalah) with a condition of option is valid because suretyship is a contract that permits options. The passage emphasizes that this is an exception to the general rule.
The period of option can be long, but the option is valid regardless.
4
Understand the basis
The general rule is that iqrar is a unilateral binding act, not a contract subject to options. Options are allowed only in contracts where khiyar is recognized by sharia (e.g., sale, suretyship). The condition is void because it contradicts the definitive nature of iqrar.
This reflects Hanafi usul: conditions that conflict with the nature of an act are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قر بشيء على أنه بالخيار لزمه بلا خيار وإن صدقه المقر له إلا إن أقر بعقد بيع وقع بالخيار له إلا إن يكذبه المقر له كإقراره بدين بسبب كفالة على أنه بالخيار في مدة ولو طويلة ا والله أعلم . قوله: (ولو أقر بتمر في قوصرة قال الرملي قال في النهاية القوصرة بالتخفيف والتشديد وعاء التمر يتخذ من قصب اهـ قوله : (وبحجلة) قال الرملي قال في الصحاح : الحجلة بيت يزين بالثياب والأسرة. قال في الجوهرة: حجلت العروس إذا اتخذت لها حجلة .اهـ. من غاية البيان قوله : (وله أن بين سبباً صالحاً) الضمير في «له» للحمل والظاهر أن محل قوله «بخلاف الإقرار للرضيع الخ». بعد هذا فتقديمه عليه سهو قوله : ( وإن ولدته ميتاً (الخ) قال الرملي : يعني إن قال المقر أوصى له به فلان ثم ولد ميتاً فإنه يرد إلى ورثة الموصي الذي قال المقر إنه أوصى للحمل. وقوله «أو ورثة أبيه : يعني إن قال المقر مات أبوه فورثه فإنه يرد إلى ورثة أبيه إن ولد ميتاً عملاً بقول المقر في المسألتين قوله : (كإقراره بدين بسبب كفالة الخ) قال الرملي قال في التبيين : هنا لأن الكفالة عقد يصح اشتراط الخيار فيه لأن الكفالة عقد يصح فيه خيار الشرط .اهـ. فليحفظ هذا .
And if he acknowledges something on the condition that he has an option, it is binding without the option, even if the acknowledgee confirms him – unless he acknowledges a sale contract that occurs with an option for him, unless the acknowledgee denies him – like his acknowledgment of a debt due to suretyship on the condition that he has an option for a period, even if long. And Allah knows best. His saying: 'And if he acknowledges dates in a basket' – al-Ramli said: In al-Nihayah, 'qusara' with tashdid and takhif is a container for dates made from reeds. His saying: 'And a bridal chamber (hujla)' – al-Ramli said: In al-Sihah, 'hujla' is a house decorated with clothes and beds. In al-Jawhara: 'hajalat al-'arus' if she takes for herself a hujla. From Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying: 'And for him if he mentions a valid cause' – the pronoun 'him' refers to the fetus. And it appears that the place of his saying 'unlike the acknowledgment for a suckling' etc. is after this; thus its precedence is an oversight. His saying: 'And if she gives birth to it dead' etc. – al-Ramli said: Meaning if the acknowledger said 'So-and-so bequeathed it to him' and then it is stillborn, it reverts to the heirs of the testator whom the acknowledger said bequeathed to the fetus. And his saying 'or the heirs of his father' means if the acknowledger said 'his father died and he inherited from him' then it reverts to the heirs of his father if stillborn, acting upon the statement of the acknowledger in both cases. His saying: 'Like his acknowledgment of a debt due to suretyship' etc. – al-Ramli said in al-Tabyin: Here because suretyship is a contract in which stipulating an option is valid, because suretyship is a contract in which the condition of option is valid. So memorize this.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage first states a ruling: if someone acknowledges something but adds a condition that he has an option (khiyar), the acknowledgment is binding and the condition is void. The exception is if he acknowledges a sale contract with an option, then the option is valid if the acknowledgee does not deny it. An example is acknowledgment of a debt due to suretyship with an option period; the option is invalid because suretyship does not allow options? Actually the commentary says suretyship does allow options, so the ruling is nuanced. The rest of the passage is a commentary (sharh) explaining terms and correcting a textual order. It clarifies that 'hujla' is a bridal chamber decorated with clothes. It also notes that the phrase 'unlike the acknowledgment for a suckling' should appear after the discussion of the fetus, not before. The commentary explains that if a fetus is acknowledged to have a bequest and is stillborn, the property returns to the bequeather's heirs, similarly if the acknowledgment is based on inheritance from the father.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): A contractual option (e.g., option of condition) that may or may not be valid in different types of acknowledgments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone acknowledges a debt of 100 dirhams but says 'on the condition that I have an option for one month,' what is the ruling?
A) The acknowledgment is void due to the invalid condition.
B) The acknowledgment is binding but the condition is void.
C) Both the acknowledgment and condition are valid.
D) The acknowledgment is binding and the condition is valid only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
A conditional option in acknowledgment is void; the acknowledgment is binding unless it arises from a sale or suretyship contract where options are valid.

Definition and Conditions of Istithna in Iqrar
Defines istithna and its condition of connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istithna in iqrar, identify its condition of connection (ittisal), and apply the exceptions to that condition.

Core Idea
An exception in an acknowledgment is a single statement affirming the remainder and negating the excepted, and it must be connected.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi legal definition of istithna (exception) in the context of iqrar (acknowledgment). You will learn how a single phrase like 'I owe you ten, except three' functions as both affirmation and negation, and why the condition of immediate connection (ittisal) is critical. The passage also clarifies what interruptions break the connection and what exceptions are allowed, such as a natural pause or calling out.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of istithna
From the passage, state what istithna means in iqrar: it is the exclusion of part of what was acknowledged, done connectedly, making the remainder binding.
The definition combines affirmation (of the remainder) and negation (of the excepted).
2
Recognize the dual semantic effect
Understand that istithna is a compound utterance: if no intent to negate the excepted, it is an indication; if intent exists, it becomes a genuine nafi and ithbat, like the kalimat al-tawhid.
The purpose of the speaker matters (qasd).
3
Apply the condition of connection (ittisal)
The exception must follow the acknowledgment immediately. Note that natural pauses (breath, cough, taking mouth) are allowed, but calling witnesses ('bear witness') between them breaks connection.
Calling someone ('O so-and-so') does not harm, as opposed to calling witnesses.
4
Identify exceptions to the connection requirement
List the permitted interruptions: catching breath, coughing, or taking something in the mouth. These do not invalidate the exception.
The passage emphasizes that these are natural and not relevant speech.
5
Distinguish harmless from harmful interruptions
Compare 'O so-and-so, except ten' (harmless) with 'You owe me a thousand, so bear witness except such-and-such' (harmful). The latter introduces a new context that severs connection.
The key is whether the interruption is unrelated to the acknowledgment or introduces a new demand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الاستثناء وما في معناه استثناء صح استثناء بعض ما أقر به متصلاً ولزمه الباقي لا حكم فيما بعد إلا بل مسكوت عند عدم القصد كمسألة الإقرار في قوله «له علي عشرة إلا ثلاثة» لفهم أن الغرض الإثبات فقط فنفي الثلاثة إشارة لا عبارة، وإثبات السبعة عكسه، وعند القصد يثبت لما بعدها نقيض ما قبلها كلمة التوحيد نفي وإثبات قصداً فالاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا باعتبار الحاصل من مجموع التركيب، ونفي وإثبات باعتبار الأجزاء. ويشترط فيه الاتصال إلا لنفس أو سعال أو أخذ فم، والنداء بينهما لا يضر كقوله یا فلان إلا عشرة بخلاف لك ألف فاشهدوا إلا كذا ونحوه.
Chapter of exception and what is in its meaning. Exception: it is valid to except part of what he acknowledged, connectedly, and the remainder is binding upon him. Exception of the whole is not valid. And the validity of excepting measured and weighed items from dirhams, not others. And if he connects his acknowledgment with 'if Allah wills', his acknowledgment is invalid. There is no ruling for what comes after [the particle 'illa'] except that it is silent when there is no intent, like the issue of acknowledgment in his statement: 'he owes me ten, except three' — understanding that the purpose is only affirmation, so the negation of three is an indication not an expression, and the affirmation of seven is the opposite. When there is intent, what follows establishes the opposite of what preceded, like the word of tawhid: negation and affirmation intentionally. So exception is speaking of the remainder after the exclusion, considering the result of the total combination, and negation and affirmation considering the parts. Connection is required except for breath, cough, or taking the mouth. Calling between them does not harm, like his saying 'O so-and-so, except ten', as opposed to 'you owe me a thousand, so bear witness except such-and-such' and the like.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage defines the legal mechanism of istithna (exception) in the context of iqrar (acknowledgment). The author first states the basic rule: it is valid to except part of what one has acknowledged, provided the exception is made connectedly (muttasilan), and the remainder is binding. The exception of the entire amount (istithna al-kull) is invalid. Then the commentary explores the semantic function of istithna: it is a compound phrase that simultaneously affirms and negates. By default, if the speaker intends merely to assert the base amount, the excepted part is not truly negated but merely indicated; if the speaker intends a true exception, then the excepted part is genuinely excluded, and the remainder is affirmed. This is analogous to the kalimat al-tawhid (la ilaha illa Allah). The key condition discussed is ittisal (connection): the exception must follow the acknowledgment immediately, without any intervening speech. Exceptions are allowed for natural pauses like breath, cough, or taking the mouth. Calling out to someone between the acknowledgment and the exception (e.g., 'O so-and-so, except ten') does not break connection, but calling witnesses ('bear witness') between them does. This is a foundational lesson for understanding how exceptions operate in Hanafi law of iqrar.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnāʾ): A legal exception where part of an acknowledgment is excluded, combining affirmation of the remainder and negation of the excepted part.
اتصال (ittiṣāl): The condition that the exception follows the acknowledgment immediately without relevant intervening speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT break the condition of connection (ittisal) between an acknowledgment and an exception?
A) Calling a witness to bear witness.
B) Calling out someone's name (e.g., 'O Zaid').
C) Saying 'bear witness' before the exception.
D) Making a separate unrelated statement.

Key Takeaway
An exception in an acknowledgment must be immediately connected to be valid, with only natural pauses and vocative calls being allowed as exceptions to the connection requirement.

Exception of the Whole (Mustaghriq) is Invalid
Ruling that exception of the whole is invalid with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a mustaghriq exception is invalid and apply it to examples in acknowledgments, wills, emancipations, and divorces.

Core Idea
An exception that swallows the entire subject is void—the full amount or effect stands.

In Hanafi law, when someone makes a statement of acknowledgment or disposition (e.g., 'I owe you 100 dirhams except 100 dirhams'), the exception is invalid if it eliminates the entire subject. This lesson explains the rule, its applications, and the key principle that it applies regardless of wording type or divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on mustaghriq
Understand that an exception consuming the entire subject is void (batil). The original statement remains fully effective.
2
Analyze the examples
Go through each example: acknowledgment of debt, will, emancipation, divorce. Note that even in revocable matters like a will, the exception is still invalid if mustaghriq.
3
Note the wording condition
If the exception wording is identical to or equivalent to the subject (e.g., 'except these' covering all), it is mustaghriq. If wording differs but still covers all, it is also invalid.
4
Recognize the no-difference rule
There is no distinction between excepting a smaller or larger amount (if it covers all) or between divisible and indivisible property. The same rule applies.
5
Apply to hypotheticals
Test with new scenarios: 'I divorce my wives except all of them' or 'This slave is free except the whole'. Conclude that the exception is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمستغرق باطل ولو فيما يقبل الرجوع كالوصية إن كان بلفظ الصدر أو مساويه وإن بغيرهما كعبيدي أحرار إلا هؤلاء أو إلا سالماً وغانماً راشداً وهم الكل، وكذا نسائي طوالق إلا فلانة وفلانة وفلانة ولا فرق بين استثناء الأقل والأكثر ولا بين ما يقسم وما لا يقسم كهذا العبد إلا ثلاثة، قوله : (كهذا العبد إلا ثلاثة لعله إلا ثلثه)
And the all-consuming [exception] is invalid, even in what admits of revocation, like a bequest, if it is with the same wording as the original or its equivalent, and if with other wording, like 'my slaves are free except these' or 'except Salim and Ghanim and Rashid' and they are all, and likewise 'my wives are divorced except so-and-so and so-and-so and so-and-so'. And there is no difference between excepting a lesser or greater [amount], nor between what is divisible and what is not, like 'this slave except three'. He said: ('like this slave except three' — perhaps it is 'except its third').
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific type of invalid exception: al-mustaghriq, where the exception completely eliminates the acknowledged matter. For example, if someone says 'I owe you one thousand dirhams except one thousand', the exception is invalid and the whole one thousand is due. The author clarifies that this rule applies even in acts that can be revoked, like a will (wasiyyah), provided the wording of the exception is identical to or equivalent to the original statement. If the wording differs, such as 'my slaves are free except these' when the exception covers all slaves, it is also invalid. The exception of the whole is void regardless of whether the amount excepted is smaller or larger (if it covers all), and whether the property is divisible or not. The marginal note corrects a possible misreading: 'this slave except three' likely means 'except its third' as the three dirhams example is for divisible property.

Key Terms
مستغرق (mustaghriq): Exception that consumes the entire subject of acknowledgment, making the exception void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone says 'I owe you 100 dirhams except 100 dirhams'?
A) The exception is valid and nothing is owed.
B) The exception is invalid and 100 dirhams are owed.
C) The exception is valid and 100 dirhams are owed.
D) The exception is invalid and nothing is owed.

Key Takeaway
An exception that entirely consumes the subject of an acknowledgment or disposition is void, and the original statement remains fully effective.

Exception of Two Numbers with a Particle of Doubt
Rule on exception with two numbers and a doubt particle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when an exception is expressed as two numbers with a particle of doubt (e.g., 'except not a hundred or fifty'), the lesser number is taken as the excepted amount, and calculate the resulting liability.

Core Idea
When doubt separates two exception figures, only the smaller is excepted.

In Hanafi law, acknowledgments with exceptions require precise interpretation. This lesson focuses on a specific scenario where the exception is phrased with two numbers and a particle of doubt, such as 'except not a hundred or fifty'. The rule simplifies: the lesser number is the exception, ensuring clarity for liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle of doubt
Look for a particle like 'aw' (or) or 'la' (not) between two numbers in the exception phrase. This indicates uncertainty.
2
Choose the lesser number as the excepted amount
The excepted amount is the smaller of the two numbers. For example, in 'except not a hundred or fifty', the exception is 50, not 100.
This is the relied-upon view (al-asahh).
3
Subtract from the acknowledged amount
Subtract the excepted amount from the acknowledged total to find the liability. E.g., acknowledged 1000, except 'not 100 or 50' → liability = 1000 - 50 = 950.
The text gives the example of 950 from an original 1000.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استثنى عددين بينهما حرف الشك كان الأقل مخرجاً نحو «له لا مائة أو خمسين لزمه تسعمائة وخمسون على الأصح وصح استثناء الكيلي والوزني والمعدود الذي لا تتفاوت آحاده كالفلوس والجوز من الدراهم والدنانير ويكون المستثنى القيمة وإن استغرقت جميع ما أقر به بخلاف دينار إلا مائة درهم فإن الاستثناء باطل لأنه مستغرق بالمساوي، وإذا كان المستثنى مجهولاً يثبت الأكثر نحو «له مائة درهم إلا شيئاً قليلاً أو بعضاً» لزمه أحد وخمسون
And if he excepts two numbers with a particle of doubt between them, the lesser is considered the excepted, like 'he owes me not a hundred or fifty' – he is liable for nine hundred and fifty according to the most correct [view]. And the exception of measured, weighed, and counted items whose individuals do not vary, like fulus and walnuts, from dirhams and dinars is valid, and the excepted is the value even if it exhausts all that he acknowledged, as opposed to 'a dinar except a hundred dirhams', for the exception is invalid because it is all-consuming by equivalence. And if the excepted is unknown, the greater [amount] is established, like 'he owes me a hundred dirhams except a little something or some' – he is liable for fifty-one.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage gives detailed rulings on exception of qiymiyat (fungible items) from dirhams and dinars. First, if the excepted amount is expressed as two numbers with a particle of doubt (e.g., 'except not a hundred or fifty'), the lower number is taken as the exception, so the liability becomes 950. The exception of measured (kayli), weighed (wazni), and counted items like fulus (copper coins) or walnuts is valid from dirhams and dinars, and the excepted amount is taken as the value (qiymah) of those items, even if that value equals the entire acknowledged sum. However, if the denomination is different (e.g., 'a dinar except a hundred dirhams'), it is mustaghriq because the dinar equals approximately 100 dirhams, making it an all-consuming exception by equivalence, and thus invalid. When the excepted is unknown (e.g., 'a little something'), the maximum possible is established: for 'a little' it is one-third of the base, so 33.33... but rounded to 33? Actually the example says 51: from 100, except 'a little' yields 51, meaning the little is 49. This needs careful explanation. The text says 'lahu mia'tu dirham illa shay'an qalilan' – he owes 100 dirhams except a little something – the excepted is unknown, so the maximum excepted is 49? Actually 100 - 49 = 51. The text states 'lazimahu ahad wa khamsun' (51). This is based on the principle that the minimum for 'a little' is 1? Wait, it's 49? I'll follow the text. So the excepted is considered the maximum possible that still leaves something, so the liable amount is 51.

Key Terms
حرف الشك (harf al-shakk): Particle of doubt, like 'أو' (or) or 'لا' (not), indicating uncertainty between two options.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debtor says, 'I owe you 500 dirhams except not 80 or 120,' what is his liability?
A) 500 dirhams
B) 420 dirhams
C) 380 dirhams
D) 300 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In an exception with two numbers and a doubt particle, the lesser number is always the excepted amount.

Exception of Measured, Weighed, and Counted Items from Dirhams and Dinars
Ruling on exception of measured/weighed/counted items from currency and the exception of one currency from another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that exception of measured, weighed, or counted items (like wheat, gold, or walnuts) from dirhams and dinars is valid, and that the excepted amount is taken as the value, even if it equals the entire acknowledged sum, and distinguish this from the invalid exception of a different currency type.

Core Idea
Exception of fungible items from currency is valid by value, but exception of one currency from another is invalid if equivalent.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in acknowledgments: when someone excepts items of a different type from money. Hanafi law permits such exceptions, treating them as exceptions of value, with a key exception when the two types are equivalent in value, making the exception void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items being excepted and the base currency
The exception involves measured (kayli), weighed (wazni), or counted (madud) items—like grains, gold, or walnuts—from a sum of dirhams or dinars.
These items are fungibles (mithliyyat or qiymiyyat) but here they are considered as value.
2
Determine the validity of the exception
The exception is valid (sahih). The excepted amount is the value (qimah) of those items at the time of the acknowledgment.
Even if that value exhausts the entire acknowledged sum, the exception is still valid.
3
Identify the invalid case
If the exception is of a different currency type that is equivalent in value (e.g., 'a dinar except a hundred dirhams'), it is invalid because it consumes the entire amount (mustaghriq).
A dinar was worth approximately 100 dirhams, so the exception leaves nothing.

Key Terms
كيلي (kaylī): Items measured by volume, like grains.
وزني (waznī): Items measured by weight, like gold and silver.
معدود (maʿdūd): Items counted individually, like eggs or walnuts.
مستغرق بالمساوي (mustaghriq bil-musāwī): All-consuming by equivalence; the exception covers the entire debt because the excepted items equal the acknowledged sum in value.

Unknown Exception: Establishing the Greater Amount
Rule for unknown exception in acknowledgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when the excepted amount is unknown (e.g., 'a little something'), the maximum possible excepted amount is considered, and calculate the resulting liability.

Core Idea
An unknown exception defaults to the maximum possible, protecting the debtor from excessive liability.

When someone acknowledges a debt but excepts an unspecified amount like 'a little' or 'some', Hanafi law interprets this as an exception of the maximum possible amount that still leaves something remaining. This prevents the debtor from exploiting vagueness to escape payment entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unknown expression
Phrases like 'a little something' (شَيء قليل) or 'some' (بعض) indicate the excepted amount is unknown.
These are ambiguous terms (mujmal).
2
Determine the maximum excepted amount
The exception is taken to be the maximum possible that still allows a remainder. For 'a little', the maximum excepted is 49 from 100, leaving 51.
The exact amount may vary with the base sum; for 100, the greater (51) is established.
3
Calculate liability
Liability = acknowledged sum - maximum excepted. For 100 dirhams except 'a little', liability = 100 - 49 = 51.
The text gives the example of 51.

Source Text

Invalidity of Conditional Acknowledgments: The Ta'liq Rule
Ruling on ta'liq in iqrar with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional acknowledgments and distinguish between void ta'liq and valid exceptions.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment suspended on an uncertain condition is void; only certain conditions or deferred terms save it.

Acknowledgment (iqrar) is a declaration that creates legal obligations. The Hanafi school requires it to be absolute and immediate. When you say 'I owe you if Allah wills' or 'if you swear', you introduce uncertainty (gharar) that defeats the purpose of acknowledgment. This lesson explains the rule and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ta'liq in iqrar: suspending an acknowledgment on a condition. The ruling is that it is batil (void) when the condition is uncertain.
2
Recognize examples of void ta'liq
Examples: linking to 'insha Allah', to another's will, or to a dangerous conditional like 'if I swear'.
Dangerous condition means it may or may not happen, creating gharar.
3
Contrast with valid exceptions
If the condition is certain to occur (e.g., 'if I die') the iqrar becomes immediate (tanjiz). If it includes a specific future date, the iqrar is valid but the term is subject to an oath.
Certain condition: death is inevitable; deferred term: 'when next month starts'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو وصل إقراره بأن شاء الله بطل إقراره وكذا بمشيئة فلان وإن شاء، وكذا كل إقرار علق بشرط على خطر ولم يتضمن دعوى أجل كان حلفت فلك ما ادعيت به وإن بشرط كائن فتنجير كعلي ألف درهم إن مت لزمه قبل الموت، وإن تضمن دعوى الأجل كإذا جاء رأس الشهر فلك علي كذا لزمه للحال ويستحلف المقر له في الأجل. ومن التعليق المبطل له ألف إلا أن يبدو لي غير ذلك أو أرى غيره أو فيما أعلم، وكذا اشهدوا أن له علي كذا فيما أعلم قوله : ( كأن حلفت فلك ما ادعيت) قال الرملي : فلو حلف لا يلزمه ولو دفع بناء على أنه يلزمه فله أن يسترد المدفوع، وسيصرح المصنف به قريباً في كتاب الصلح في فصل في صلح الورثة بقوله : ولو قال المدعى عليه إن حلفت أنها لك دفعتها فحلف المدعي ودفع المدعى عليه الدراهم إن كان دفع له بحكم الشرط فهو باطل وللدافع أن يسترد اهـ. وقدمنا شيئاً من مسائل تعليق الإقرار في باب دعوى الرجلين .اهـ.
His saying: (And if he connects his acknowledgment with 'if Allah wills', his acknowledgment is invalid, and similarly with 'by the will of so-and-so' and 'if he wills', and likewise every acknowledgment suspended on a dangerous condition and does not include a claim of a term, like 'if I swear, then you have what you claim' — but if with a condition that is certain, it is immediate like 'I owe you a thousand dirhams if I die' is binding before death. And if it includes a claim of a term, like 'when the beginning of the month comes, you have such-and-such upon me', it is binding immediately and the acknowledgee is sworn regarding the term. And from the nullifying suspension: 'he owes me a thousand except that it becomes apparent to me otherwise' or 'if I see otherwise' or 'as far as I know', and likewise 'bear witness that he has such-and-such upon me as far as I know'. His saying: (like 'if I swear, you have what you claim') — al-Ramli said: If he swears, it is not binding upon him, and if he pays on the basis that it is binding, he may reclaim what he paid. The author will soon state this clearly in the book of Sulh in the chapter on the Sulh of heirs: 'If the defendant said: if I swear that it is yours, I will pay it, then the plaintiff swore and the defendant paid the dirhams — if he paid due to the condition, it is invalid and the payer may reclaim.' End. And we have previously presented some issues of suspending acknowledgment in the chapter of the claim of two men. End.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage deals with the invalidity of iqrar when it is suspended on a condition that introduces uncertainty (gharar). The primary example is linking the acknowledgment to 'insha Allah' (if Allah wills), which renders the acknowledgment void. Similarly, any condition that makes the acknowledgment contingent on a future uncertain event, without establishing a deferred claim (da'wa ajjal), invalidates it. For instance, 'if I swear, you have what you claim' is a void suspension. However, if the condition is certain to occur (e.g., 'if I die'), the iqrar is binding immediately (tanaijur), meaning the debt is due now, not upon death. If the acknowledgment includes a specific future date (e.g., 'when the month begins'), the iqrar is valid and the debt is due at that time, but the acknowledgee must be sworn regarding the term. The text also includes a marginal note from al-Ramli clarifying that if such a conditional acknowledgment leads to payment, the payer may reclaim it because the condition was void. This is consistent with the principle that iqrar must be absolute and unconditional to be valid.

Key Terms
تعليق (taʿlīq): Suspending an acknowledgment on a condition, making it conditional.
تنجيز (tanjīz): Immediate effect; opposite of ta'liq.
خطر (khatar): Danger or uncertainty; a condition that may or may not occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acknowledgments is valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'I owe you 100 dirhams if Allah wills.'
B) 'I owe you 100 dirhams when I die.'
C) 'I owe you 100 dirhams if you swear.'
D) 'I owe you 100 dirhams as far as I know.'

Key Takeaway
An acknowledgment must be absolute; any suspension on an uncertain condition invalidates it, except when the condition is certain or a fixed term is specified.

Recovery of Payment Made Under Void Conditional Acknowledgment
Ruling on reclaiming money paid under void conditional acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a payer under a void conditional acknowledgment can reclaim the paid amount.

Core Idea
Payment made due to a void conditional acknowledgment may be recovered; the condition is not binding.

The previous lesson established that conditional acknowledgments are void. But what if someone pays based on such an acknowledgment? The text provides a ruling from al-Ramli and the author: the payer can reclaim the money because the condition is invalid. This lesson focuses on the remedial aspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person makes a void conditional iqrar (e.g., 'if I swear, you have the claim'). The acknowledgee demands payment, and the person pays.
2
Determine the legal effect of the condition
The condition is void; the iqrar is invalid. No obligation arises from it.
3
Apply the ruling on payment
If payment is made despite the void iqrar, it is not a valid discharge of a debt. The payer may reclaim the amount because the payment was not due.
This is analogous to payment under duress or mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: (And if he connects his acknowledgment with 'if Allah wills', his acknowledgment is invalid, and similarly with 'by the will of so-and-so' and 'if he wills', and likewise every acknowledgment suspended on a dangerous condition and does not include a claim of a term, like 'if I swear, then you have what you claim' — but if with a condition that is certain, it is immediate like 'I owe you a thousand dirhams if I die' is binding before death. Validity of Exception: Ornament, Lining, and Building
Rulings on exception of parts from composite objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid exceptions of specific parts (ornament, lining, building) from composite objects in Hanafi law, and explain the role of custom (ʿurf) in determining integral parts.

Core Idea
An exception is invalid when the excepted part is integral to the object by custom, such as the lining of a robe or the building on land.

In Hanafi law, the validity of an exception (istithna) from an acknowledgment depends on whether the excepted part is considered a separate entity by custom. The following passage from Baḥr al-Rāʾiq examines classic cases: ornament on a sword, lining on a robe, and building on land. The rulings hinge on the degree to which the part is integrated into the whole.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the excepted part
Read the acknowledgment and note what is being acknowledged and what part is excepted (e.g., 'This sword is mine except its ornament').
The exception may be valid or invalid.
2
Determine integrality by custom (ʿurf)
Assess whether the excepted part is considered an integral component of the whole by customary practice. For instance, the lining of a robe is integral, while the ornament on a sword is not.
Custom is the decisive factor.
3
State the ruling
If the part is integral, the exception is invalid (fasid) and the entire object belongs to the acknowledgee. If not integral, the exception is valid and the excepted part remains with the acknowledger.
Examples: building from house → invalid; ornament from sword → valid.
4
Apply analogies
Apply the same principle to analogous cases: collar on slave girl (invalid), gem on ring (invalid), palm tree in garden (invalid) – all considered integral.
The text explicitly compares these to the building case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحلية في السيف والظهارة والبطانة في الجبة لا يلزمه شيء واستثناء البيت من الدار صحيح ولو استثنى البناء من الدار فهما للمقر له والطوق في الجارية والفص في الخاتم والنخلة في البستان نظير البناء والإقرار بالحائط والاسطوانة إقرار بما تحتهما من الأرض إلا إذا كانت من خشب وبناؤها لي والعرصة لفلان فهو كما قال وبناؤها لي وأرضها لفلان فهما لفلان وبناؤها لزيد وأرضها لعمرو فلكل ما أقر له به وفي عكسه الكل للأول كقوله هذه الدار لفلان وهذا البيت لي وأرضها لي وبناؤها لفلان فعلى ما أقر ويؤمر المقر له بنقل البناء من أرضه والأصل أن الدعوى قبل الإقرار لا تمنع صحة الإقرار بعده والدعوى بعد الإقرار في بعض ما دخل تحته غير صحيحة وأن إقراره حجة عليه فقط وفي التتارخانية عن المنتقى : إذا قال لغيره هذا الخاتم لي إلا فصه فإنه لك أو هذه المنطقة إلا حليتها فلك أو هذا السيف إلا حليته أو إلا حمائله فلك أو هذه الجبة لي إلا بطانتها فلك والمقر له يقول هذه الجبة لي فالقول على ما أقر به المقر ثم بنظر إن لم يكن في نزع المقر به ضرر للمقر يؤمر بنزعه والدفع وإلا أجبر المقر بقيمة ما أقر به وهذا كله قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف اهـ وفيها قبل ما مر : لو قال هذا البستان لفلان إلا النخلة بغير أصولها فإنها لي لا يصح الاستثناء بخلاف إلا نخلها بأصولها وكذلك هذه الجبة لفلان إلا بطانتها لأن البطانة تدخل في البيع تبعاً فكانت كالبناء ثم قال وهو محمول على جبة بطانتها في النفاسة دون الظهارة وكذلك لو قال هذا السيف لفلان إلا حليته لا الاستثناء
And the ornament on the sword, the outer lining and inner lining on the robe: nothing is due from him. Exception of the house from a house is valid. If he excepts the building from the house, both are for the acknowledgee. The collar on the slave girl, the gem on the ring, the palm tree in the garden are analogous to the building. Acknowledgment of a wall and a pillar is acknowledgment of what is beneath them of the ground, unless they are of wood. 'Its building is mine and the courtyard is for so-and-so': it is as he said. 'Its building is mine and its land is for so-and-so': both are for so-and-so. 'Its building is for Zayd and its land is for Amr': each has what he acknowledged for him. In the opposite, all is for the first, like his saying 'this house is for so-and-so, and this room is mine, and its land is mine, and its building is for so-and-so' – it is according to what he acknowledged, and the acknowledgee is ordered to transfer the building from his land. The principle is that a claim before acknowledgment does not prevent the validity of the acknowledgment after it, and a claim after acknowledgment regarding part of what is included is not valid, and his acknowledgment is evidence against him only. And in al-Tatarkhaniyah from al-Muntaqa: If he said to another, 'this ring is mine except its gem, it is for you', or 'this belt except its ornament is for you', or 'this sword except its ornament or its strap is for you', or 'this robe is mine except its lining is for you', and the acknowledgee says 'this robe is mine', the word is as the acknowledger said. Then it is considered: if there is no harm to the acknowledger in removing what he acknowledged, he is ordered to remove it and deliver; otherwise, the acknowledger is forced to pay the value of what he acknowledged. This is all the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them. And in it before the above: If he said, 'this garden is for so-and-so except the palm tree without its roots, it is mine', the exception is not valid, as opposed to 'except its palm tree with its roots'. Similarly, 'this robe is for so-and-so except its lining' because the lining is included in the sale as a subsidiary, thus like the building. Then he said: It is applied to a robe whose lining is of luxurious fabric, not the outer lining. Similarly, if he said 'this sword is for so-and-so except its ornament', the exception is not valid.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This lengthy passage addresses the validity of exception when it involves parts of a composite object. The key principle is whether the excepted part is considered a separate entity by custom ('urf). For example, if someone acknowledges a sword but excepts its ornament (hilyah), the ornament is not automatically included in the acknowledgment; however, the exception is valid. For a robe (jubbah), the outer lining (dhiharah) and inner lining (batinah) are considered part of the robe, so excepting them is not valid unless stated otherwise. Exception of a house (bayt) from a house (dar) is valid because a house is a distinct part. Exception of a building (bina') from a house is not valid because the building is integral; both are for the acknowledgee if he excepts it. Analogous to building are: the collar (tawq) on a slave girl, the gem (fass) on a ring, and the palm tree (nakhla) in a garden. For a wall or pillar, acknowledging them includes the ground beneath unless they are made of wood. The passage also provides rules for conflicting claims about ownership of building and land: if the building is declared for Zayd and the land for Amr, each is for the one acknowledged. If the building is claimed by A and the land by B, the building owner is ordered to remove it unless it causes damage. The quote from al-Tatarkhaniyah elaborates on similar cases, noting the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that the word of the acknowledger is taken, and if removal is possible without harm, it is ordered; otherwise, compensation is paid. Special cases include exception of a palm tree without roots (invalid) and with roots (valid), and exception of lining when the lining is of high quality.

Key Terms
حلية (ḥilyah): Ornament or decoration, e.g., on a sword or belt.
بطانة (biṭānah): Inner lining of a garment.
بناء (bināʾ): Building or structure on land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone says 'this house is for so-and-so, but the building is mine'?
A) The building is mine and the land is for so-and-so.
B) Both the building and the land are for so-and-so.
C) The building is mine and the land is mine.
D) The acknowledgee is forced to remove the building.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exception depends on whether the excepted part is considered integral by custom; integral parts cannot be excepted.

8 min
Exception with Harm and Special Cases (Palm Trees, Luxurious Linings)
Rulings on harm and special exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that if removal of an excepted part causes harm, the acknowledger must compensate its value, and identify special exceptions for palm trees and luxurious linings based on custom.

Core Idea
When a valid exception would require removal that damages the whole object, compensation replaces physical removal.

The previous lesson established basic validity rules for exceptions of parts. However, the passage from Baḥr al-Rāʾiq, via al-Tatarkhāniyah, introduces an important practical nuance: if removing the excepted part would cause harm to the larger object, the acknowledger is not forced to remove it but must pay its value. This lesson also covers two special cases: the palm tree with or without roots, and the luxurious lining of a robe, where custom dictates the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of a valid exception
Assume the exception is valid (the part is not integral). Consider whether physical removal of the excepted part would cause harm (ḍarar) to the main object.
Harm means substantial damage or decrease in value.
2
Apply the harm rule
If no harm: the acknowledger is ordered to remove and deliver the excepted part. If harm: the acknowledger is forced to pay the value (qīmah) of the excepted part, and the part remains with the main object.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Apply the special rule for palm trees
If the excepted part is a palm tree without roots (bighayri uṣūlihā), the exception is invalid because the tree without roots is not considered a separate entity. If excepted with roots (bi-uṣūlihā), the exception is valid.
Roots make the tree a distinct part.
4
Apply the special rule for linings
The exception of the inner lining (biṭānah) from a robe is generally invalid because the lining is integral. However, this applies specifically to a robe with a luxurious inner lining (biṭānah fī al-nafāsah). For ordinary linings or the outer lining (dhihārah), the ruling may differ.
Custom determines 'luxurious'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For a robe (jubbah), the outer lining (dhiharah) and inner lining (batinah) are considered part of the robe, so excepting them is not valid unless stated otherwise. Exception of a house (bayt) from a house (dar) is valid because a house is a distinct part. Exception of a building (bina') from a house is not valid because the building is integral; both are for the acknowledgee if he excepts it. Analogous to building are: the collar (tawq) on a slave girl, the gem (fass) on a ring, and the palm tree (nakhla) in a garden. For a wall or pillar, acknowledging them includes the ground beneath unless they are made of wood. The passage also provides rules for conflicting claims about ownership of building and land: if the building is declared for Zayd and the land for Amr, each is for the one acknowledged. If the building is claimed by A and the land by B, the building owner is ordered to remove it unless it causes damage. The quote from al-Tatarkhaniyah elaborates on similar cases, noting the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that the word of the acknowledger is taken, and if removal is possible without harm, it is ordered; otherwise, compensation is paid. Special cases include exception of a palm tree without roots (invalid) and with roots (valid), and exception of lining when the lining is of high quality.

Key Terms
ضرر (ḍarar): Harm or damage; in this context, substantial damage to the object if the excepted part is removed.
نفاسة (nafāsah): Luxurious or high quality, referring to a lining that is valuable and integral to the garment's worth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone validly excepts the gem from a ring, but removing the gem would break the ring, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The gem must be removed regardless of damage.
B) The acknowledger must pay the value of the gem.
C) The exception is invalid because it causes harm.
D) The ring is given to the acknowledgee with the gem.

Key Takeaway
When a valid exception would require removal that harms the object, compensation replaces physical delivery.

Rulings on Exception (Istithna) in Iqrar
Rulings on valid/invalid exceptions in confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the validity of exceptions in confessions of property and apply the principle that an exception is valid only if the excepted item is a separable part.

Core Idea
An exception in a confession is valid only if the excepted item is a separable part of the whole.

When a person confesses ownership of property but adds an exception, the validity of that exception depends on whether the excepted item can be separated by custom or nature. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules to determine when such exceptions hold and when they fail, protecting legal certainty in property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confession and its exception
Locate the act of iqrar (confession) and the phrasing of the exception (istithna). Note the two parts: the subject of confession and what is excluded.
E.g., 'This house is yours except the building.'
2
Determine if the excepted item is separable
Apply the Hanafi principle: an exception is valid only if the excepted part can be physically or legally separated from the whole. Inseparable items (e.g., building from house, gem from ring) cannot form a valid exception.
Customary practice ('urf) determines separability.
3
Apply the ruling
If the exception is invalid, the confession applies to the entire property without reduction. If valid, the confession applies only to what remains after the exception.
Valid exception: 'this house except the ground floor' — separable.
4
Handle conditional and ambiguous exceptions
Exceptions that are conditional ('unless I see otherwise') or based on uncertain knowledge ('as far as I know') are invalid because they introduce uncertainty.
Such phrases nullify the exception, making the confession absolute.
5
Note the principles of timing
A claim made before the confession does not invalidate the confession; but a claim after the confession regarding part of what was confessed is not accepted. The confession is binding only against the confessor.
These principles protect the integrity of the act of iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإقرار باب الاستثناء وما في معناه استثنى البناء من الدار فهما للمقر له وإن قال بناؤها لي والعرصة لك فكما قال ولو قال علي ألف من ثمن عبد لم أقبضه فإن عين العبد وسلمه إليه لزمه الألف وإلا لا وإن لم يعين لزمه الألف كقوله من ثمن خمر أو خنزير ولو قال من ثمن متاع أو أقرضني ومن التعليق المبطل له ألف إلا أن يبدو لي غير ذلك أو أرى غيره أو فيما أعلم، وكذا اشهدوا أن له علي كذا فيما أعلم والحلية في السيف والظهارة والبطانة في الجبة لا يلزمه شيء واستثناء البيت من الدار صحيح ولو استثنى البناء من الدار فهما للمقر له) والطوق في الجارية، والفص في الخاتم والنخلة في البستان نظير البناء والإقرار بالحائط والاسطوانة إقرار بما تحتهما من الأرض إلا إذا كانت من خشب (وبناؤها لي والعرصة لفلان فهو كما قال) وبناؤها لي وأرضها لفلان فهما لفلان وبناؤها لزيد وأرضها لعمرو فلكل ما أقر له به، وفي عكسه الكل للأول كقوله هذه الدار لفلان وهذا البيت لي وأرضها لي وبناؤها لفلان فعلى ما أقر ويؤمر المقر له بنقل البناء من أرضه والأصل أن الدعوى قبل الإقرار لا تمنع صحة الإقرار بعده، والدعوى بعد الإقرار في بعض ما دخل تحته غير صحيحة وأن إقراره حجة عليه فقط .
Book of Confession, Chapter on Exception and Its Meanings: If he excepts the building from the house, then both belong to the confesse. If he says 'its building is mine and the plot is yours,' it is as he said. If he says 'I owe a thousand from the price of a slave I did not receive' — if he specified the slave and handed it over, the thousand is binding; otherwise not. If unspecified, the thousand is binding, like saying 'from the price of wine or pork.' If he says 'from the price of merchandise' or 'he lent me.' From invalid conditional statements: 'He owes a thousand unless it appears otherwise to me' or 'unless I see otherwise' or 'as far as I know.' Similarly, 'Bear witness that he owes me such and such as far as I know.' The ornament on the sword, the outer lining and inner lining of the gown — nothing is binding. Exception of the house from a house is valid. If he excepts the building from the house, both go to the confesse. The necklace of the slave girl, the gem of the ring, the date palm in the orchard are analogous to the building. Confession of a wall or a pillar is a confession of the ground beneath them unless they are of wood. 'Its building is mine and the plot for so-and-so' — it is as he said. 'Its building is mine and its land for so-and-so' — both are for so-and-so. 'Its building for Zayd and its land for Amr' — each gets what he confessed for. In the opposite case, all is for the first, e.g., 'This house is for so-and-so, this house is mine, its land is mine, its building is for so-and-so' — according to what he confessed, and the confesse is ordered to move the building from his land. The principle: A claim before confession does not prevent the validity of a subsequent confession; a claim after confession regarding part of what it includes is invalid; his confession is a proof only against him.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings on the validity of exceptions (istithna) in confessions (iqrar). The text begins with examples: if someone says 'I own this house except the building,' the exception is invalid and both the building and the house belong to the confesse. The same applies to analogous items like the ornament of a sword, the lining of a gown, the gem of a ring, and the date palm in an orchard, because these are inseparable parts by custom. The passage clarifies that exception of the building from a house fails because the building cannot be separated; if both are claimed, they go to the confesse. It then moves to conditional exceptions ('unless I see otherwise') which are invalid because they make the obligation uncertain. A key principle is stated: a prior lawsuit does not prevent a later valid confession, but a lawsuit after confession regarding part of what was confessed is not accepted. The confession is binding only on the confessor, not on others.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): An exception made during a confession, e.g., 'this house is yours except the building.'
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor the confession is made.
البناء (al-binā'): The building constructed on land, treated as a single unit with the land for confession purposes.
التعليق المبطل (al-ta'liq al-mubtil): An invalid conditional clause that nullifies the exception, e.g., 'unless I see otherwise.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is not binding because the slave was not received.
B) The debt is binding only if the slave is handed over later.
C) The debt is binding immediately because the price reference does not affect the obligation.
D) The confession is void due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
An exception in a confession is valid only if the excepted item is separable; otherwise, the confession applies to the whole property.

Rulings on Exception (Istithna) in Acknowledgment
Covers rulings on exception in acknowledgments of debt and property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on valid exception in acknowledgment and apply it to scenarios involving debts, deposits, and property.

Core Idea
An exception in acknowledgment is only valid if connected to the statement; otherwise the full acknowledgment stands.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, when a person acknowledges a debt or property, adding an exception can reduce or alter the liability. However, not all exceptions are accepted—the key is whether the exception is made immediately after the acknowledgment (connected) or later (disconnected). This lesson examines the rules governing such exceptions, including special cases like counterfeit coins and conflicting claims between deposit and usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Classify the acknowledgment
Determine whether the acknowledgment concerns a debt, sale, deposit, usurpation, etc. Different types have different rules for exceptions.
Debt and property acknowledgments are treated differently regarding exception.
2
Evaluate the exception’s connection
If the exception is stated immediately after the main acknowledgment (muttasil), it is valid; if later (munfasil), it is rejected. Check the wording and timing.
The exception must be in the same breath or sentence.
3
Apply the rule on coin quality
If a debt is acknowledged as good dirhams, claiming they were counterfeit or defective does not relieve the confiter from paying good ones, except in contracts like deposit or usurpation.
In ghasb and wadi'a, the confiter is believed regarding defect.
4
Analyze conflicting claims
When the confiter says ‘deposit’ and confitee says ‘usurpation’, liability depends on wording. If confiter says ‘I took as deposit and it perished’, he is guarantor; if he says ‘you gave me as deposit’, he is not.
The distinction hinges on who initiated the taking.
5
Resolve multiple confitees
If one acknowledges that property is a deposit for two different persons, the property goes to the first, and the confiter owes the second an equivalent.
Priority is given to the first confitee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإقرار باب الاستثناء وما في معناه وهي زيوف أو نبهرجة لزمه الجياد بخلاف الغصب والوديعة ولو قال إلا أنه ينقص كذا متصلاً صدق وإلا لا ومن أقر بغصب ثوب وجاء بمعيب صدق وإن قال أخذت منك ألفا وديعة وهلكت وقال أخذتها غصباً فهو ضامن وإن قال أعطيتنيها وديعة وقال غصبتنيها لا وإن قال هذا كان وديعة لي عندك فأخذته فقال هو لي أخذه وإن قال إذا صدقه أو أقام بينة. ولو قال إني اشتريت منه مبيعاً إلا أني لم أقبضه قبل قوله كما قبل قول البائع بعته هذا ولم أقبض الثمن والمبيع في يد البائع ولو قال له علي ألف درهم حرام أو ربا فهي لازمة مطلقاً، لو قال زوراً أو باطلاً لزمه إن كذبه المقر له وإلا فلا. والإقرار بالبيع تلجئة على هذا التفصيل ولو أقر بالشراء أو الإجارة أو الهبة أو الصدقة وقال لم أقبض صدق موصولاً كان أو مفصولاً، ولو أقر بالمسلم ثم قال لم أقبض رأس المال لا يصدق إلا إذا كان موصولاً كالوديعة والقرض بخلاف دفعت إلي أو نقدتني وقال لم أقبض لا يصدق مطلقاً بخلاف أعطيتني إن وصل ولو أقر بثمن مبيع أو قرض من النقود أو الفلوس ثم ادعى أنها زيوف أو نبهرجة أو ستوقة أو رصاص أو كاسدة الزمه الجياد وإن وصل ويتحالفان في البيع حال قيام السلعة بخلاف الغصب والوديعة والمضاربة فإنه يصدق في الزيوف والنبهرجة مطلقاً، وفي الستوقة إن وصل وكان حياً ولا يصدق وارثه بعد موته ويصدق في دعوى الرداءة في المكيل والموزون الثمن أو القرض. ولو قال له ألف درهم زيوف فهي كما قال على الأصح كقوله له علي كذا إلا أنها وزن خمسة ونقد البلد وزن سبعة أو إلا أن ينقص كذا متصلا ولو قال له علي عشرة جياد إلا خمسة زيوفاً لزمه خمسة جياد ويصير مستثنياً من العشرة خمسة جياداً ومن أقر بغصب ثوب وجاء بمعيب صدق وإن قال أخذت منك ألفاً وديعة وهلكت وقال أخذتها غصباً فهو ضامن بخلاف أخذتها قرضاً أو بيعاً أو قال أعطيتنيها وديعة فقال غصبتنيها لا يضمن المقر ولو قال هذا كان وديعة لي عندك فأخذته فقال هو لي أخذه إن كان قائماً وقيمته إن كان هالكاً، وكذا أقرضتك ألفاً ثم أخذتها منك ولو قال أجرت بعيري أو ثوبي هذا فلاناً فركبه أو لبسه فرده فالقول للمقر ولو قال هذا الألف وديعة فلان لا بل وديعة لفلان فالألف للأول وعلى المقر مثله للثاني.
Book of Acknowledgment. Chapter of Exception and what is in its meaning. If they are counterfeit or defective, good ones are binding upon him, unlike usurpation and deposit. If he said except that it decreases by such and such, connected, he is believed, otherwise not. Whoever acknowledges a usurpation of a garment and came with a defective one, he is believed. If he said 'I took from you a thousand as a deposit and it perished' and [the other] said 'I took it as usurpation' then he is guarantor. If he said 'You gave it to me as a deposit' and [the other] said 'You usurped it from me' no [guarantee]. If he said 'This was a deposit of mine with you so I took it' and he said 'It is mine' he takes it if he confirms him or brings proof. If he said 'I bought from him a commodity except that I did not receive it' his statement is accepted just as the seller's statement 'I sold this and did not receive the price and the commodity is in the seller's hand.' If he said 'I owe a thousand dirhams as forbidden or usury' it is binding absolutely. If he said 'false or invalid' it is binding if the confitee denies him, otherwise not. Acknowledgement of sale as a screen is upon this detail. If he acknowledged purchase or lease or gift or charity and said 'I did not receive' he is believed whether connected or disconnected. If he acknowledged a salam then said 'I did not receive the capital' he is not believed unless connected, like deposit and loan, unlike 'you paid to me' or 'you tendered to me' and he said 'I did not receive' he is not believed absolutely, unlike 'you gave me' if connected. If he acknowledged the price of a sale or a loan of coins or copper coins then claimed they were counterfeit or defective or lead or obsolete, he is forced to give good ones even if connected, and they take an oath in a sale while the commodity is present, unlike usurpation, deposit, and mudaraba, for he is believed regarding counterfeit and defective absolutely, and regarding lead if connected and he is alive, and his heir is not believed after his death, and he is believed in claiming deficiency in measured and weighed items, whether price or loan. If he said 'I owe a thousand dirhams, counterfeit' it is as he said as the most correct view, like his saying 'I owe such and such except that its weight is five and the currency of the country is seven' or 'except that it decreases by such and such' connected. If he said 'I owe ten good dirhams except five counterfeit' he is bound to pay five good dirhams, and he becomes an exception from ten of five good dirhams. Whoever acknowledges a usurpation of a garment and came with a defective one, he is believed. If he said 'I took from you a thousand as a deposit and it perished' and he said 'I took it as usurpation' then he is guarantor, unlike if he said 'I took it as a loan or a sale' or 'you gave me as a deposit' and he said 'you usurped it from me' the confiter does not guarantee. If he said 'this was a deposit of mine with you so I took it' and he said 'it is mine' he takes it if it exists and its value if destroyed, similarly if he says 'I lent you a thousand then I took it from you.' If he said 'I leased or loaned my camel or this garment of mine to so-and-so so he rode or wore it, then returned it' the statement is of the confiter. If he said 'this thousand is a deposit of so-and-so, no rather a deposit of so-and-so' then the thousand belongs to the first and upon the confiter its like for the second.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents rulings on exception (istithna) in acknowledgments, particularly regarding debts and property. The author begins by stating that if one acknowledges a debt of good dirhams but then claims they were counterfeit (ziyuf) or defective (nubharaja), good ones are binding, unlike in cases of usurpation (ghasb) or deposit (wadi'a) where the confiter is believed. Exception to an acknowledgment is only valid if connected (muttasil) to the statement. Various scenarios illustrate this: in acknowledgment of sale without receipt, the confiter's word is accepted; in salam, only if connected. For debts of coins, claiming they are defective does not excuse payment of good ones unless in specific contracts like ghasb or wadi'a. When the confiter says 'except that it decreases by such and such' and it is connected, he is believed. The passage also covers cases of multiple acknowledgments with exception, as when one says 'I owe ten good dirhams except five counterfeit' — he owes five good dirhams. The ruling on acknowledgment of usurpation of a garment: if the garment is returned defective, the confiter is believed as to the defect. The difference between saying 'I took as deposit' and 'I took as usurpation' is explained: in the former case, if the confiter acknowledges deposit but the confitee claims usurpation, the confiter is not guarantor; but if the confiter acknowledges taking a deposit and claims it perished, and the confitee says it was taken as usurpation, the confiter is guarantor. Finally, if one acknowledges that a property is a deposit of two different persons, the property goes to the first confitee, and the confiter owes the second an equivalent.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): Exception in acknowledgment; a statement that excludes some portion from the acknowledged liability.
زيوف (ziyūf): Counterfeit coins; not genuine currency.
نبهرجة (nubharaja): Defective or irregular coins; of poor quality.
متصلاً (muttasilan): Connected; the exception is stated immediately after the acknowledgment without interruption.
منفصلاً (munfasilan): Disconnected; the exception is stated separately after the acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Five good dirhams
B) Ten good dirhams
C) Five dirhams, but they can be counterfeit
D) Nothing, because the exception is invalid

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exception in acknowledgment hinges on its connection to the main statement; without it, the full acknowledgment is binding.
