Rulings on Acknowledgment by a Sick Person
Rules for acknowledgments made during terminal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules governing a terminally ill person's acknowledgment of debt, including priority among debts and the prohibition of preferring some creditors over others.

Core Idea
A sick person's debt acknowledgment is valid but subordinate to health debts; all creditors share proportionally.

When a person is terminally ill, his acknowledgment of debts carries special considerations to protect creditors and heirs. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq outlines how such acknowledgments are treated: they are valid and executed from the entire estate, but they do not override debts from the period of health or liabilities arising from known causes. The sick person cannot arbitrarily favor one creditor over another, ensuring equitable treatment among all creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
Recognize that the sick person's acknowledgment of a debt is effective (nāfidh) from all his wealth, and inheritance is postponed until debts are settled.
This establishes the validity of the acknowledgment.
2
Determine Priority of Debts
Debts incurred during health (dīn al-ṣiḥḥa) and obligations arising from known causes during illness (e.g., marriage, sale, tort) take priority over mere acknowledgments made during fatal illness.
A known cause (sabab ma'rūf) excludes gifts.
3
Understand the Prohibition of Preferring Creditors
The sick person may not pay some creditors and ignore others; all must be treated equally, including dower and hire payments.
Exception: repayment of a loan taken during illness or payment for a purchase made during illness, if proven by evidence.
4
Recognize Proportional Sharing
If the sick person acknowledges multiple debts, the creditors share the estate proportionally (taḥāṣṣā), whether the acknowledgments were connected or disconnected.
This applies when the estate is insufficient for all debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب إقرار المريض إقراره بدين نافذ من كل ماله وأخر الإرث عنه ودين الصحة وما لزمه في مرضه بسبب معروف قدم على ما أقر به في مرض موته ولو وديعة والسبب المعروف كالنكاح بمهر المثل، والبيع المشاهد والإتلاف كذلك وغيرها مما ليس من التبرعات، وليس له أن يقضي دين بعض الغرماء دون بعض ولو إعطاء مهر وإيفاء أجرة إلا إذا قضى ما استقرض في مرضه أو نقد ثمن ما اشترى فيه وقد علم ذلك بالبيئة بخلاف ما إذا لم يؤد حتى مات فإن البائع أسوة فالغرماء إذا لم تكن العين في يده . وإذا أقر بدين ثم بدين تحاصا وصل أو فصل.
Chapter of Acknowledgment by the Sick Person. His acknowledgment of a debt is effective from all his wealth, and inheritance is delayed. And the debt of health and what became binding upon him in his illness due to a known cause is given priority over what he acknowledged in his fatal illness, even if a deposit. And the known cause is like marriage with a proper dower, observed sale, destruction likewise, and other things not being gifts. And he may not pay the debt of some creditors over others, even if it is payment of dower and fulfillment of hire, except if he paid what he borrowed in his illness or paid the price of what he purchased in it, and that is known by evidence, unlike if he did not pay until he died, then the seller is equal [to other creditors]. So the creditors [are equal] if the property is not in his hand. And if he acknowledged a debt then [another] debt, they share proportionally, whether connected or disconnected.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the special rulings for acknowledgments made by a person during his terminal illness (marad al-mawt). The primary principle is that such an acknowledgment of debt is valid and executed from the entire estate, but it is subordinate to debts incurred during health. Specifically, debts from the period of health and liabilities arising from known causes during illness (such as marriage with proper dower, observable sale, or torts) take priority over acknowledgments made in the fatal illness. The sick person cannot prefer one creditor over another; all share proportionally. However, if during the illness he repays a loan he took in that illness or pays the price for a purchase made in that illness, and that is proven by evidence, that payment is valid and not subject to the rule of equality. But if he dies before paying, the seller becomes an equal creditor. Additionally, if the sick person acknowledges one debt then another, both creditors share proportionally whether the acknowledgments were connected or disconnected. This ensures justice among creditors and prevents the sick person from favoring some over others through arbitrary acknowledgments.

Key Terms
إقرار المريض (iqrār al-marīḍ): Acknowledgment of a debt by a person in terminal illness; subject to special rules to protect creditors.
سبب معروف (sabab ma'rūf): A known cause for liability, such as marriage, sale, or destruction; not a gift.
تحاص (taḥāṣṣā): They share proportionally; creditors divide the estate proportionally according to their debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which debt takes priority over a debt acknowledged during fatal illness?
A) A debt acknowledged during fatal illness
B) A deposit acknowledged during illness
C) A debt incurred during health
D) All debts are equal

Key Takeaway
In terminal illness, debt acknowledgments are valid but subordinate to health debts, and all creditors share proportionally without preference.

Iqrār of a Sick Person: Child vs Sibling Lineage
Explains conditions and effects of iqrār for nasab and spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of iqrār of a child and iqrār of a sibling, and apply the conditions for valid iqrār of a spouse.

Core Idea
Iqrār of a child binds all heirs; iqrār of a sibling only binds the acknowledger.

The Hanafi school gives detailed rules for a sick person's acknowledgment (iqrār) regarding lineage and marriage. A key distinction is between acknowledging a direct descendant (child) versus a collateral relative (sibling). This affects inheritance rights and the binding nature on other heirs. The passage also specifies conditions for acknowledging a spouse and a freed slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage focuses on iqrār (acknowledgment) by a sick person. The general ruling is that such iqrār is valid and affects the heirs.
2
Distinguish between iqrār of a child and iqrār of a sibling
Note that iqrār of a child (or parent, spouse, mawla) is general: it establishes lineage universally, so the acknowledged person inherits from the deceased's father and shares with all heirs even if they deny. In contrast, iqrār of a sibling is specific: it only binds the acknowledger, meaning the sibling does not inherit from the father and only shares from the acknowledger's share.
This is the core distinction: umum vs khusus in the effect of iqrār.
3
Identify conditions for iqrār of a spouse
For a valid iqrār of a wife, the woman must not be currently married to another, not be in her iddah period, and the acknowledger must not have her sister among his wives nor four other wives besides her.
These ensure no conflict with existing marital relationships.
4
Understand the role of confirmation (tasdiq)
Confirmation by the acknowledged person is generally required, except for a child who cannot speak. For a slave, the master must confirm. Confirmation after the death of the acknowledger is usually valid, except a husband's confirmation after his wife's death.
5
Apply the distinction to inheritance scenarios
If a sick person acknowledges a child, the child inherits from the father and shares with all heirs. If he acknowledges a brother, the brother only inherits from the acknowledger's share if he has no other heir; otherwise, the brother does not inherit at all from the father.
This has practical implications for estate distribution.

Source Text

كتاب الإقرار باب إقرار المريض مريضاً ويشارك الورثة وصح إقراره بالولد والوالدين والزوجة والمولى وإقرارها بالوالدين والزوج والمولى وبالوالد إن شهدت قابلة أو صدقها زوجها ولا بد من تصديق هؤلاء وصح التصديق بعد موت المقر لا تصديق الزوج بعد موتها وإن أقر بنسب نحو الأخ والعم لم يثبت فإن لم يكن له وارث غيره قريب أو بعيد ورثه وإن كان مات أبوه فأقر بأخ شاركه في الإرث ولم يثبت نسبه وإن ترك ابنين وله على آخر مائة فأقر أحدهما بقبض أبيه خمسين منها فلا شيء للمقر وللآخر خمسون. Book of Iqrār: Section on Iqrār of the Sick Person. A sick person's iqrār is valid and it shares with the heirs. His iqrār of a child, parents, wife, and mawla is valid, and her iqrār of parents, husband, and mawla, and of a father if a midwife testifies or her husband confirms her. Confirmation from these is necessary. Confirmation after the death of the acknowledging person is valid, except confirmation by the husband after her death. And if he acknowledges a lineage like a brother or uncle, it is not established. But if he has no other heir, near or distant, that person inherits from him. And if his father died and he acknowledges a brother, that brother shares in the inheritance but his lineage is not established. And if he leaves two sons and owes another one hundred, and one of the sons acknowledges that his father received fifty of it, then nothing is for the acknowledging one, and the other gets fifty. (And) it is valid unless the acknowledging person has established lineage from another, then the acknowledged person has that description there. (And his iqrār of a child, parents, with the preceding conditions is valid), (and the wife) if she is free from a husband and her waiting period, and the acknowledging person does not have her sister under him nor four others besides her. (And the mawla) from the direction of emancipation if his wala' is not established from another. (Her iqrār of other than a child, and of a child if a midwife testifies or the husband confirms her) if she has a husband or is in waiting period, and absolutely if she is not like that or if she claims he is from someone else. (And it is necessary that the acknowledging person be confirmed in all except the child if he cannot express himself. And if the acknowledged person is a slave belonging to another, the confirmation of the master is required. (And confirmation after the death of the acknowledging person is valid except confirmation by the husband after her death. And if he acknowledges lineage upon another like a brother, uncle, grandfather, and grandson, it is not valid concerning others, but it is valid concerning himself, so that the rulings of maintenance, custody, and inheritance become binding upon him if they both affirm it. Then if he has no other heir, he inherits; otherwise no. And the difference between the two positions is in two aspects: first, that lineage is established in iqrār like that of a child generally, so the iqrār transfers to others beyond the acknowledging person, so that if he acknowledges a son, he inherits and shares with his heirs even if they deny him, and he inherits from the father of the acknowledging person who is the grandfather of the acknowledged person even if the grandfather denies his being a son of his son. And it invalidates marriage if a woman of unknown lineage acknowledges that she is the daughter of her husband's father if the father confirms her. And in iqrār like that of a brother specifically, there is no sharing for the acknowledged brother with his heirs if they deny, and he does not inherit from the father due to acknowledgment. As for the validity of the second, the acknowledging person cannot revoke like that of a child, but it is valid like that of a brother, so that if he acknowledges a brother and confirms him, then revokes what he acknowledged, then bequeaths all his wealth to someone, it is all for the legatee.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the iqrār of a sick person regarding lineage and marital relationships. The text begins by stating that a sick person's iqrār is valid and will affect the heirs. It then details the specific cases: iqrār of a child, parents, wife, and a freed slave (mawla) are valid under certain conditions. For a woman, her iqrār of a father is valid only if a midwife testifies or her husband confirms. The passage emphasizes that confirmation from the acknowledged parties is necessary, except for a child who cannot speak. It also addresses iqrār of siblings: acknowledging a brother or uncle does not establish full lineage rights for inheritance from the deceased's estate beyond the acknowledging person's share. The key distinction is that iqrār of a child binds all heirs, whereas iqrār of a sibling only binds the acknowledging person, meaning the sibling does not inherit from the father of the deceased if other heirs deny. The text then provides a detailed explanation of the difference, highlighting that iqrār of a child is general (binding on all) while iqrār of a sibling is specific (only binding on the one who acknowledges). Finally, it clarifies that once a person acknowledges a sibling and confirms it, he cannot revoke that acknowledgment.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): An acknowledgment or admission of a right or fact, especially regarding lineage or debt, by a person.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or parentage, the legal relationship of descent.

Definition and Basic Ruling of Sulh (Compromise)
Defines sulh and states its validity and legal treatment in three scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of sulh, identify the three scenarios in which it is valid, and explain when it is treated as a sale and when it is invalidated by ignorance.

Core Idea
Sulh is a dispute-resolving contract valid even with denial, but when it involves property with acknowledgment, it takes the full rules of a sale.

Sulh, or compromise, is a crucial mechanism in Islamic law to settle disputes amicably. The Hanafi school provides a precise definition and outlines when it is valid and what legal consequences follow. In this lesson, we dissect the classical definition from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and understand its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Read the Arabic definition: 'A contract that removes dispute'. Understand that the primary purpose of sulh is to end a conflict between parties.
This definition sets sulh apart from other contracts like sale or lease.
2
Recognize the three valid scenarios
The text states sulh is valid with iqrār (acknowledgment), sukut (silence), or inkār (denial). Each scenario has a different legal effect.
Sulh is not restricted to cases where the defendant admits the claim.
3
Understand the sale treatment when iqrār and property exchange
If sulh involves property-for-property and is based on the defendant's acknowledgment, it is treated as a sale. This triggers all sale-related rights: pre-emption, return for defect, option of inspection, and option of condition.
The underlying transaction is re-characterized as a sale for legal purposes.
4
Note the invalidating factor: jahāla (ignorance)
The passage ends with 'ignorance invalidates it.' This means if there is significant uncertainty about the subject matter that can lead to further dispute, the sulh is void.
Jahāla here refers to excessive ignorance that defeats the purpose of resolving dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح عقد يرفع النزاع وهو جائز بإقرار وسكوت وإنكار فإن وقع عن مال بمال بإقرار اعتبر بيعاً فيثبت فيه الشفعة والرد بالعيب وخيار الرؤية والشرط وتفسد جهالة
Book of Sulh: A contract that removes dispute, and it is valid with acknowledgment, silence, and denial. If it occurs for property in exchange for property with acknowledgment, it is considered a sale, so the right of pre-emption, return due to defect, option of inspection, and option of condition are established in it, and ignorance invalidates it.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage introduces the Book of Sulh (compromise or settlement). It defines sulh as a contract that removes a dispute, and states that it is valid in three scenarios: when the defendant acknowledges the claim (iqrār), when he remains silent (sukut), or when he denies the claim (inkār). The text then specifies that if the sulh involves an exchange of property for property and is based on acknowledgment, it is treated as a sale. This means all the subsidiary rules of a sale apply: the right of pre-emption (shuf'a), the right to return the item due to a defect (radd bi al-ayb), the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya), and the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). Additionally, ignorance (jahala) regarding the subject matter invalidates the sulh if it leads to further dispute.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract aimed at resolving a dispute between parties through mutual compromise.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment of the claim by the defendant.
سكوت (sukut): Silence of the defendant, neither admitting nor denying the claim.
إنكار (inkār): Denial of the claim by the defendant.
جهالة (jahāla): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter of the sulh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is sulh treated as a sale?
A) When it is based on denial
B) When it is based on silence
C) When it is based on acknowledgment and involves property for property
D) When it involves a right other than property

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a versatile dispute-resolution contract valid in three scenarios, and when it involves property exchange with acknowledgment, it becomes a sale with all attendant rights and obligations.

Sulh: Definition, Conditions, and Invalidating Ignorance
Definition and conditions of sulh with ruling on ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sulh, identify its essential conditions, and determine when ignorance invalidates the contract.

Core Idea
Sulh resolves disputes by transferring claims, but only if the subject is known and the right given up is exchangeable.

Sulh (compromise) is a flexible contract that can settle disputes over property, debts, or even retaliation (qisas). However, its validity depends on precise conditions. In this passage, the commentator unpacks the definition, cause, pillars, conditions, and the critical rule that ignorance leading to dispute invalidates the contract.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Identify the core phrase: 'sulh is a contract that removes dispute.' Understand its purpose.
The definition is concise; note that it covers all forms of dispute.
2
Analyze cause and pillars
Find that the cause (sabab) is like other transactions – capability to undertake. Pillars (rukn) are offer and acceptance.
These are standard for contracts.
3
List the conditions
Note two key conditions: musalah alayh must be known if delivery required; musalah anhu must be a right that permits substitution.
Exclusions: shuf'ah, qadhf, kafalah bil-nafs cannot be musalah anhu.
4
Understand the ruling on ownership
Ownership vests in claimant for musalah alayh; for musalah anhu, if property and admission, ownership passes to defendant; otherwise absolution.
This distinguishes admission vs. denial.
5
Apply the rule of ignorance
Jahalah that leads to dispute invalidates if it involves something needing delivery; otherwise not.
Example: unspecified property leads to invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح هو عقد يرفع النزاع وسببه سبب المعاملات تعلق المقدور بتعاطيه، وركنه الإيجاب والقبول الموضوعان له شرطه كون المصالح عليه معلوماً إن كان يحتاج إلى قبضه والمصالح عنه حقاً يجوز الاعتياض عنه ولو غير مال كالقصاص معلوماً كان أو مجهولاً لا ما لا يجوز الاعتياض عنه كحق الشفعة وحد القذف والكفالة بالنفس وطلب الصلح كاف عن القبول من المدعى عليه إن كان المدعى به مما لا يتعين بالتعيين وإن كان مما يتعين فلا بد من قبول المدعى عليه ويشترط شرائط ذلك العقد الملحق به من بيع وإجارة. وحكمه في جانب المصالح عليه وقوع الملك فيه للمدعى، سواء كان المدعى عليه مقراً أو منكراً. وفي المصالح عنه وقوع الملك فيه للمدعى عليه إن كان مما يحتمل التمليك كالمال وكان المدعي عليه مقراً به، وإن كان مما لا يحتمل التمليك كالقصاص ووقوع البراءة كما إذا كان منكراً مطلقاً. والجهالة فيه إن كانت تقضي إلى المنازعة كوقوعها فيما يحتاج إلى التسليم منعت صحته وإلا لا فبطل إن كان المصالح عليه أو عنه مجهولاً يحتاج إلى التسليم كصلحه بعد دعواه مجهولاً على أن له مالاً ولم يسمه
The Book of Sulh: It is a contract that removes dispute. Its cause is like the cause of transactions: attachment of capability to its undertaking. Its pillar is offer and acceptance that are placed for it. Its condition is that the object of settlement (musalah alayh) be known if it requires delivery, and the subject of settlement (musalah anhu) be a right that permits substitution, even if non-property like qisas, known or unknown; not something that does not permit substitution like right of preemption, hadd of slander, surety of person. Request for sulh suffices in place of acceptance from the defendant if the claimed item is not specified by specification; but if it is specified, then acceptance of the defendant is necessary. Conditions of the contract to which it is attached, like sale and lease, are required. Its ruling on the side of musalah alayh: ownership vests in the claimant, whether the defendant is admitting or denying. On the side of musalah anhu: ownership vests in the defendant if it is something that admits transfer of ownership like property and the claimant admits to it; but if it does not admit transfer of ownership like qisas, then absolution occurs, as when the defendant is absolutely denying. Ignorance in it: if it leads to dispute, like when it occurs in something requiring delivery, it prevents its validity; otherwise no. So it becomes invalid if musalah alayh or musalah anhu is unknown and requires delivery, like his sulh after claiming an unknown item that he has property without specifying it.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It defines sulh as a contract that removes dispute (raf' al-niza'). The author mentions its cause (sabab) as attachment of capability to its undertaking, its pillars (arkan) as offer and acceptance, and conditions (shurut) that the musalah alayh be known if it requires delivery, and the musalah anhu be a right permissible for substitution. Rights that cannot be waived or exchanged for compensation are excluded. The ruling establishes ownership transfer to the claimant for musalah alayh, and to the defendant for musalah anhu when he admits and it is property; otherwise absolution. Ignorance (jahalah) leading to dispute invalidates the contract if the item needs delivery.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract that removes dispute (رفع النزاع) between parties
مصالح عليه (musalah alayh): The property or benefit that is being settled upon and transferred to the claimant
مصالح عنه (musalah anhu): The right or claim that is being given up or exchanged for the settlement
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following rights can be musalah anhu according to the passage?
A) Right of preemption (shuf'ah)
B) Hadd of slander (hadd al-qadhf)
C) Qisas (retaliation)
D) Surety for a person (kafalah bil-nafs)

Key Takeaway
Sulh is a valid dispute resolution contract provided the transferred item is known if deliverable, and the right given up is legally exchangeable; ignorance causing dispute invalidates it.

Sulh After Denial: The Binding Effect of Settlement
Rulings on sulh after denial and post-settlement effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of sulh and apply the rule that post-sulh admission and evidence do not overturn the settlement.

Core Idea
Once sulh is concluded, subsequent admission or evidence by the defendant does not invalidate the settlement.

This lesson covers the first part of the passage, which clarifies that sulh is permissible whether the defendant acknowledges the claim, remains silent, or denies it. We then focus on the critical rule: after a sulh based on denial, any later admission or evidence by the defendant is not binding—the settlement stands final. This ensures the finality of sulh and prevents endless litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three bases for sulh
The text states sulh is valid with iqrar (acknowledgement), sukut (silence), and inkar (denial). Recognize that each is a legitimate starting point for a settlement.
Sulh with denial is the most common in disputes.
2
Analyze the effect of post-sulh admission
If the defendant denies, makes sulh, then later admits the claim, the admission is not binding. Understand that the sulh has already resolved the matter; new admissions do not reopen it.
This prevents the defendant from undermining the settlement.
3
Analyze the effect of post-sulh evidence
Similarly, if after sulh the defendant presents evidence to prove his innocence, it is not accepted. The sulh is conclusive.
The exception: evidence that the claimant admitted lack of right before the sulh is accepted.
4
Distinguish the exception
If the defendant brings evidence that the claimant admitted before the sulh that the defendant owed nothing, that evidence is accepted because it shows the claimant's prior admission, not a new fact.
This is not a reopening of the sulh but a proof of pre-sulh acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو جائز بإقرار وسكوت وإنكار فلو أنكر ثم صالح ثم أقر لا يلزمه ما أقر به، وكذا لو أقام بينة بعد صلحه لا تقبل، ولو أقام بينة على إقرار المدعي أنه لا حق له قبله قبل الصلح أو قبل القبض والصلح بعد الحلف لا يصح كالصلح مع المودع بعد دعوى الاستهلاك، وصلح الأب عند مال الصبي جائز كيفما كان إن لم يكن له بينة وإلا لا
And it is permissible with acknowledgement, silence, and denial. So if he denies, then makes sulh, then admits, what he admitted is not binding upon him. Similarly, if he presents evidence after his sulh, it is not accepted. If he presents evidence that the claimant admitted that he has no right against him before the sulh or before taking possession, [it is accepted?] and sulh after taking an oath is not valid, like sulh with a depositary after claiming misappropriation. The sulh of a father with the property of a minor is valid in whatever manner, unless he has evidence; otherwise, no.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage enumerates specific cases for sulh after the initial stage. It establishes that sulh is permissible in three states: acknowledgement, silence, and denial. If the defendant denies the claim, then makes sulh, and later admits the claim, his admission does not bind him because the sulh already settled the matter. Similarly, if after sulh the defendant produces evidence that he was right, it is not accepted—the sulh stands. However, if he produces evidence that the claimant had admitted before the sulh that the defendant owed nothing, that evidence is accepted because it shows the claimant acknowledged lack of right before the contract. Sulh after taking an oath (after the defendant's oath denying the claim) is not valid, like the case of a depositary who claims misappropriation; sulh with him after that is invalid. For the father settling a minor's property, his sulh is valid in any manner unless he has evidence against the minor; if he has evidence, it is not valid (because he would be giving up a proven right).

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the defendant that the claimant's claim is true
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually witness testimony, presented in court
صلح (sulh): Settlement contract to end a dispute

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a defendant, after making sulh (settlement) while denying the claim, later admits the claim?
A) The admission is binding and the sulh is void
B) The admission is not binding; the sulh stands
C) The sulh is valid but the admission must be honored
D) The claimant can choose to accept the admission or the sulh

Key Takeaway
Sulh after denial is final; subsequent admission or evidence by the defendant does not overturn the settlement.

Sulh After Oath and the Guardian's Sulh for a Minor
Rulings on sulh after oath and guardian's sulh for minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi internal dispute on sulh after oath and explain the conditions for a father's sulh of a minor's property.

Core Idea
Sulh after oath is invalid per one narration but mufta bihi is valid; father's sulh for minor is valid unless he has evidence against the minor.

This lesson covers two additional cases from the passage: sulh after the defendant has taken an oath denying the claim, and the father's sulh concerning the property of his minor child. The first case involves an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf; the second sets a condition for the guardian's authority to settle.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on sulh after oath
The text says 'sulh after taking an oath is not valid, like sulh with a depositary after claiming misappropriation.' Understand that this is the reported position from Imam Muhammad.
But there is a stronger position that it is valid.
2
Identify the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad narrates from Abu Hanifa that such sulh is invalid. However, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad himself (in his own view?) hold it valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is validity, as per al-Hamawi.
This ikhtilaf is recorded in al-Ashbah.
3
Apply the father's sulh rule
The father's sulh with the property of a minor is valid in any manner, unless he has evidence (against the minor). If he has evidence, the sulh is not valid because he would be giving up a proven right.
The father must not prejudice the minor's rights.
4
Differentiate the two cases
Sulh after oath has a dispute but is ultimately accepted; father's sulh has a clear condition: no evidence against the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو جائز بإقرار وسكوت وإنكار فلو أنكر ثم صالح ثم أقر لا يلزمه ما أقر به، وكذا لو أقام بينة بعد صلحه لا تقبل، ولو أقام بينة على إقرار المدعي أنه لا حق له قبله قبل الصلح أو قبل القبض والصلح بعد الحلف لا يصح كالصلح مع المودع بعد دعوى الاستهلاك، وصلح الأب عند مال الصبي جائز كيفما كان إن لم يكن له بينة وإلا لا
And it is permissible with acknowledgement, silence, and denial. So if he denies, then makes sulh, then admits, what he admitted is not binding upon him. Similarly, if he presents evidence after his sulh, it is not accepted. If he presents evidence that the claimant admitted that he has no right against him before the sulh or before taking possession, [it is accepted?] and sulh after taking an oath is not valid, like sulh with a depositary after claiming misappropriation. The sulh of a father with the property of a minor is valid in whatever manner, unless he has evidence; otherwise, no.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage enumerates specific cases for sulh after the initial stage. It establishes that sulh is permissible in three states: acknowledgement, silence, and denial. If the defendant denies the claim, then makes sulh, and later admits the claim, his admission does not bind him because the sulh already settled the matter. Similarly, if after sulh the defendant produces evidence that he was right, it is not accepted—the sulh stands. However, if he produces evidence that the claimant had admitted before the sulh that the defendant owed nothing, that evidence is accepted because it shows the claimant acknowledged lack of right before the contract. Sulh after taking an oath (after the defendant's oath denying the claim) is not valid, like the case of a depositary who claims misappropriation; sulh with him after that is invalid. For the father settling a minor's property, his sulh is valid in any manner unless he has evidence against the minor; if he has evidence, it is not valid (because he would be giving up a proven right).

Key Terms
صلح بعد الحلف (sulh ba'd al-hilf): Sulh concluded after the defendant has taken an oath denying the claim
مستودع (mustawda'): Depositary (person entrusted with keeping something)
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually witness testimony

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on sulh after the defendant's oath?
A) It is invalid.
B) It is valid.
C) It is valid only if the claimant also takes an oath.
D) It is invalid unless the subject is property.

Key Takeaway
Sulh after oath is valid according to the operative fatwa; father's sulh for minor is valid only if he lacks evidence.

Sulh as Sale: When Does Property-for-Property Compromise Become a Sale?
Treating sulh over property for property with acknowledgment as a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sulh over property with acknowledgement is considered a sale, and apply the Hanafi rules regarding same-genus settlements and the two exceptions.

Core Idea
Sulh with acknowledgement is a sale only if the item is of a different genus; otherwise it is remission, receipt, or usury.

In Hanafi fiqh, sulh (compromise) can take many forms. When it involves exchanging property for property with the defendant's acknowledgment, it closely resembles a sale. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the critical exceptions, and the legal effects that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states that if sulh occurs over property for property with acknowledgement, it is considered a sale (i'tubira bay'an). This is the baseline rule.
The key elements: 'property for property' and 'acknowledgment' (iqrar) from the defendant.
2
Distinguish by genus
The sale treatment applies only when the settlement item is of a different genus than the original claim. If it is the same genus, different rules apply.
'Different genus' means the subject matter is not the same type as the original debt.
3
Note the two exceptions
Exception 1: If a creditor settles a debt for a slave, the creditor cannot resell that slave without declaring the origin. Exception 2: If both parties later agree that no debt existed, the sulh is void.
These exceptions prevent abuse and preserve legal certainty.
4
Understand same-genus treatment
If the settlement is of the same genus: (a) less than claim → reduction and remission (hatt wa ibra'); (b) equal → receipt and collection (qabd wa istifa'); (c) more → usury (riba).
Same-genus sulh is not a sale; it is a discharge or exchange of the debt itself.
5
Apply sale effects and istihqaq
When treated as a sale, all sale rules apply: pre-emption (shuf'ah), defect return (radd bi al-'ayb), option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah). Also ignorance of term or price invalidates. If the settled property is claimed by a third party (istihqaq), the defendant recovers proportional consideration.
These rules ensure consistency with standard sale law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وقع عن مال بمال بإقرار اعتبر بيعاً فيثبت فيه الشفعة والرد بالعيب وخيار الرؤية والشرط وتفسد جهالة أجله وبدله قوله : (فإن وقع عن مال بمال بإقرار اعتبر بيعاً) إن كان على خلاف الجنس إلا في مسألتين: الأولى إذا صالح من الدين على عبد وصاحبه مقر بالدين وقبض العبد ليس له المرابحة من غير بيان . الثانية إذا تصادقا على أن لا دين بطل الصلح كما لو استوفى عين حقه ثم تصادقا أن لا دين، فلو تصادقا على أن لا دين لا يبطل الشراء، وإن وقع على جنسه فإن كان بأقل من المدعي فهو حط وإبراء، وإن كان بمثله فهو قبض واستيفاء، وإن كان بأكثر فهو ربا، وإذا اعتبر بيعاً ثبتت أحكامه فيثبت به الشفعة والرد بالعيب وخيار الرؤية ويفسده جهالة الأجل والبدل) إن كان مما يحتاج إلى التسليم وإن استحق بعض المصالح عنه أو كله رجع المدعى عليه بحصة ذلك من العوض أو كله ولو استحق المصالح عليه أو بعضه رجع بكل المصالح عنه أو ببعضه
If it occurs over property for property with acknowledgement, it is considered a sale, so pre-emption, defect return, option of seeing, and condition become established, and ignorance of its term and price invalidates it. His saying: '(If it occurs over property for property with acknowledgement, it is considered a sale)' — if it is of a different genus, except in two issues: First: if he settles a debt for a slave and his companion admits the debt, and he takes possession of the slave, he does not have the option of resale without clarifying. Second: if they agree that there is no debt, the sulh is void, just as if he collected the very same right and then they agree there is no debt; if they agree there is no debt, the purchase is not void. If it occurs of its own genus, then if less than the claim, it is reduction and remission; if equal, it is receipt and collection; if more, it is usury. And when it is considered a sale, its rules apply: pre-emption, defect return, option of seeing are established, and ignorance of term and price invalidates it if it is something requiring delivery. And if some or all of the musalah anhu is claimed back (istihqaq), the defendant recovers his share of the consideration or all of it. And if the musalah alayh or some of it is claimed back, he recovers all of the musalah anhu or some of it.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage details the classification of sulh when it involves property for property with the defendant's acknowledgment. In such a case, the sulh is treated as a sale (bay'), provided the settlement item is of a different genus than the original claim. The author then provides two exceptions: (1) settling a debt for a slave — here the creditor cannot resell the slave without disclosing the origin; (2) if both parties agree there was no debt originally, the sulh is void (unlike a straightforward purchase where later agreement of no debt does not void the purchase). If the settlement is of the same genus, it is treated either as reduction (if less), receipt (if equal), or usury (if more). As a sale, all sale rules apply: pre-emption (shuf'ah), defect return (radd bi al-'ayb), option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'yah), and it is invalidated by ignorance of the term or price. Finally, if the subject matter is claimed back by a third party (istihqaq), the defendant recovers the consideration proportionally, and vice versa.

Key Terms
اعتبر بيعاً (i'tubira bay'an): It is legally considered a sale; all sale rules apply
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption right of a co-owner to purchase the share sold
استحقاق (istihqaq): Third-party claim of ownership that proves the subject matter belonged to someone else
حط وإبراء (hatt wa ibra'): Reduction and remission; partial forgiveness of debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In sulh over property for property with acknowledgement, if the settlement item is of a different genus than the original claim, what is the ruling?
A) It is considered a gift (hibah)
B) It is considered a sale (bay')
C) It is considered a lease (ijarah)
D) It is considered a loan (qard)

Key Takeaway
Sulh over property with acknowledgement is effectively a sale when the settlement is of a different genus, with two exceptions and special rules for same-genus settlements.

Sulh as Lease: Rules and Conditions
Treating sulh over property for benefit as a lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a sulh over property for a benefit is classified as a lease (ijarah) and apply the relevant conditions (timing vs specification) and invalidators (death, loss of subject matter, killing of slave by master) while distinguishing the exception where the benefit matches the claimed right.

Core Idea
Sulh for a benefit is a lease; it requires timing or benefit specification, and is voided by death or destruction of the subject matter.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains that when a sulh (compromise) involves property in exchange for a benefit (manfa'ah), the Hanafi school treats it as an ijarah (lease) contract. Therefore, all the rules of ijarah apply, including conditions for validity and events that invalidate it. The passage also clarifies special scenarios like the killing of a slave and importantly draws a line: if the benefit received is exactly what the claimant originally claimed, then it is not a lease but partial collection of the claim. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal equivalence
Recognize that the passage establishes: 'sulh over property for a benefit = ijarah.' This means all ijarah rules apply, including conditions (shurut) and invalidators (mufsidat).
2
Extract the conditions for the benefit
Note that for time-based benefits (service of a slave, dwelling in a house), timing (tawqit) is required. For task-based benefits (dyeing a garment, riding an animal, carrying food), specification of the benefit (not necessarily timing) is the condition.
The distinction is based on whether the benefit is inherently time-bound or can be defined by the action itself.
3
Identify the invalidators (mufsidat)
Find that the contract is voided by: (a) death of either party if the contract was for themselves, (b) loss of the subject matter (fawat al-mahal) before full utilization. In case of partial loss, the contract is void for the remainder, and the claimant returns the consideration proportionally.
Loss can be destruction of the item or its benefit – e.g., slave dies, house burns.
4
Analyze the slave-killing scenario
If the sulh was for the service of a slave and the slave is killed: if the master (owner) kills, the contract is void with no liability on the master. If a third party kills, the master is liable for the value and may buy a substitute slave. Contrast with a mortgaged slave and a bequest of service.
The key difference: in mortgage, the master is liable for destruction and emancipation; in bequest, the master can buy a substitute like here.
5
Recognize the exception: benefit matching the claimed right
The lease classification only applies when the benefit is different from the original claim. If the claimant claims a house and settles for living in it for a month, that dwelling is part of his right, not a new lease. Therefore, the defendant's leasing of the house to the claimant later is valid (as a separate contract).
This prevents double-counting: the claimant cannot both receive his right and treat it as a separate lease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وقع من مال بمنفعة اعتبر إجارة فثبت أحكامها فيشترط التوقيت) فيما يحتاج إليه كخدمة العبد وسكنى الدار بخلاف صبغ الثوب وركوب الدابة وحمل الطعام فالشرط بيان تلك المنفعة ( وتبطل بموت أحدهما إن عقدها لنفسه، وكذا بفوات المحل قبل الاستيفاء، ولو كان بعد استيفاء البعض بطل فيما بقي ويرجع بالمدعي بقدره ولو كان الصلح على خدمة عبد فقتل إن كان القاتل المولى بطل وإلا ضمن قيمته واشترى بها عبداً يخدمه إن شاء كالموصى بخدمته بخلاف المرهون حيث يضمن المولى بالإتلاف والعتق . وإنما يعتبر إجارة إذا وقع على خلاف المدعى به، فإن ادعى داراً فصالحه على سكناها شهراً فهو استيفاء لبعض حقه لا إجارة فتصح إجارته للمدعى عليه
And if it occurs over property for a benefit, it is considered a lease, so its rules apply: timing is required for what needs it, like service of a slave and dwelling in a house, unlike dyeing a garment, riding an animal, and carrying food—here the condition is the specification of that benefit. It becomes void by the death of either party if he contracted for himself, and also by the loss of the subject matter before utilization. If after partial utilization it becomes void in the remainder, and the claimant returns [the consideration] proportionally. If the sulh was over the service of a slave and he is killed: if the killer is the master, the contract is void; otherwise, he [the master] is liable for his value and buys a slave to serve him if he wishes, like the case of a bequest of service, unlike the mortgaged slave where the master is liable for destruction and emancipation. It is only considered a lease if it occurs contrary to the claimed right; for if he claims a house and settles for dwelling in it for a month, it is collection of part of his right, not a lease, so his leasing it to the defendant is valid.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage deals with sulh where the settlement is over property in exchange for a benefit (manfa'ah). In this case, the sulh is treated as a lease (ijarah). The rules of ijarah apply: timing is required if the benefit is time-based (like service or dwelling), but if the benefit is specific (like dyeing or transport), specification suffices. The contract becomes void by death of either party if contracted for themselves, or by destruction of the subject matter. If the subject matter is partially utilized and then destroyed, the contract is void for the remainder with proportional refund. If the subject matter is a slave's service and the slave is killed, the ruling depends on who killed: if the master killed, the contract is void without liability; if another killed, the master collects compensation and may buy another slave to fulfill the service. This differs from a mortgaged slave where the master is liable. Importantly, this lease classification only applies if the benefit is different from the claimed right; if the benefit is exactly what was claimed (e.g., claiming a house and settling for its dwelling), it is considered partial receipt of the claim, not a lease.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): A lease contract for a benefit in exchange for consideration
توقيت (tawqit): Specification of time period for the benefit
فوات المحل (fawat al-mahal): Loss or destruction of the subject matter of the contract
خدمة (khidmah): Service, e.g., of a slave
سكنى (sukna): Dwelling, residing
صبغ (sabgh): Dyeing (a garment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a sulh for a benefit (e.g., service of a slave) if the slave is killed by the master?
A) The contract remains valid, and the master must provide another slave
B) The contract becomes void, and the master is not liable
C) The contract becomes a sale (bay')
D) The claimant gets the value of the slave

Key Takeaway
Sulh over property for a benefit is a lease with specific conditions and invalidators, but it is not a lease if the benefit matches the claimed right.

Sulh over Denial: Fida' and Mu'awadah Classification
Explains the classification and consequences of sulh over denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi classification of sulh over silence or denial as fida' al-yameen for the denier and mu'awadah for the claimant, and state the consequences regarding spot exchange, shuf'ah, and the distinction between legal and moral absolution.

Core Idea
Sulh over denial ransoms the oath for the denier and is a transaction for the claimant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the dual nature of sulh when the defendant remains silent or denies the claim. Understanding this classification helps determine the fiqh rules that follow: the requirement of mutual possession before separation, the application of pre-emption, and the gap between legal and moral innocence.

Guided Path
1
Classify the sulh scenario
Identify whether the sulh is over silence (sukut) or denial (inkar). For the denier, it is fida' al-yameen (ransom for the oath); for the claimant, it is mu'awadah (transaction).
The classification depends on each party's perspective.
2
Apply the spot exchange rule
Since mu'awadah is a transaction of property, if the sulh involves the same genus (e.g., dirhams for dirhams), it must be a spot exchange. If the parties separate before possession, the sulh is void.
This follows the rules of sarf (currency exchange).
3
Determine shuf'ah applicability
When each party transfers their own property to the other, it is a mu'awadah and shuf'ah does not apply. But if one party pays a price for the other's property, shuf'ah applies.
Shuf'ah attaches to sales, not to settlements that are not sales.
4
Distinguish legal and moral effects
Legally, the defendant is absolved in court, but if he was actually obligated, he is not morally absolved unless the claimant explicitly releases him. Similarly, the claimant may not keep the settlement if he was lying.
Qada' (judicial) absolution does not affect dianah (religious duty).
5
Handle istihqaq (third-party claim)
If the subject of sulh is reclaimed by a rightful owner, the claimant pursues that owner and returns the consideration. If only part is reclaimed, proportional return is required.
If the reclaimed item is of the same genus as the claim, the sulh remains valid and the claimant recovers the equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلح عن سكوت أو إنكار فداء لليمين في حق المنكر ومعاوضة في حق المدعي) فبطل الصلح على دراهم بعد دعوى دراهم إذا تفرقا قبل القبض (فلا شفعة إن صالحا عن داريهما ويجب لو صالحا على داريهما ولا يحل للمدعي ما أخذه إن كان كاذباً ولا يبرأ المدعى عليه كذلك مما عليه وإن برئ قضاء إلا إذا أبرأه المدعي عما بقي (ولو استحق المتنازع فيه رجع المدعي بالخصومة) المستحق (ورد البدل ولو بعضه فبقدره ولو استحق المصالح مع عليه أو بعضه رجع إلى الدعوى في كله أو بعضه إلا إذا كان مما لا يتعين بالتعيين وهو من جنس المدعى به فحينئذ يرجع بمثل ما استحق ولا يبطل الصلح كما إذا ادعى ألفاً فصالحه على مائة وقبضها فإنه يرجع عليه بمائة عند استحقاقها، سواء كان الصلح بعد الإقرار أو المنح عن السراجية وحكى القولين في القنية قال الحموي في حاشية الأشباه : ما مشى عليه في الأشباه رواية محمد عن أبي حنيفة وما مشى عليه في البحر قولهما وهو الصحيح كما في معين المفتي
And sulh over silence or denial is a ransom for the oath with respect to the denier and a transaction with respect to the claimant. So the sulh over dirhams after a claim of dirhams becomes void if they separate before possession. So there is no pre-emption if they settle for both of their houses; but it becomes necessary if they settle on both of their houses. And it is not permissible for the claimant to take what he took if he was lying, and the defendant is similarly not absolved from what he owes, though he is absolved judicially, unless the claimant absolves him from what remains. And if the subject of dispute is claimed back (istihqaq), the claimant returns to the lawsuit against the rightful owner and returns the consideration; if some of it, then proportionally. And if the musalah alayh or some of it is claimed back, he returns to the lawsuit in whole or part, except if it is something not specified by specification and it is of the same genus as the claim; then he returns by the like of what was claimed back and the sulh does not become void, as when he claims a thousand and settles for a hundred and takes possession; then he recovers from him a hundred when it is claimed back, whether the sulh was after acknowledgement or denial. From al-Sirajiyyah. And he reported two views in al-Qunyah. Al-Hamawi said in his Hashiyat al-Ashbah: What is followed in al-Ashbah is the narration of Muhammad from Abu Hanifah, and what is followed in al-Bahr is the view of the two (Sahibayn), and it is correct as in Mu'in al-Mufti.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This final passage classifies sulh when it is based on silence (sukut) or denial (inkar). For the denying defendant, the sulh is a 'ransom for the oath' (fida' al-yameen) because it prevents the claimant from taking an oath to prove his claim. For the claimant, it is a transaction (mu'awadah) because he receives something in place of his claim. Consequently, if the sulh is over the same genus of money (e.g., dirhams for dirhams), it must be a spot exchange; otherwise, if they separate before possession, it is invalid (like a sale of currency without possession). Regarding pre-emption (shuf'ah): if they settle over both houses (i.e., each transfers their own house to the other), there is no pre-emption because it is not a sale; but if they settle on both houses (i.e., one gives his house to the other for a price), pre-emption applies. Morally, the claimant may not keep what he took if he was lying (it is haram), and the defendant is not relieved of his actual obligation despite the legal absolution, unless the claimant explicitly absolves him. In case of istihqaq (third-party claim), the claimant pursues the rightful owner and returns the consideration. The end of the passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding sulh after oath: in al-Ashbah it is considered valid (narration of Muhammad from Abu Hanifa), while in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq it is invalid (view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, i.e., Sahibayn). Al-Hamawi states that the correct position for fatwa is the view of the Sahibayn (valid).

Key Terms
فداء اليمين (fida' al-yameen): Ransom of the oath: the sulh prevents the oath from being taken, so it is like buying off the oath
معاوضة (mu'awadah): Transaction: the claimant receives something in exchange for his claim
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption right to purchase a co-owned share

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in this passage, is sulh after the defendant's oath valid?
A) Yes, it is valid
B) No, it is invalid
C) It depends on the type of claim
D) It is valid only if the claimant is honest

Key Takeaway
Sulh over denial is a dual contract: fida' for the denier, mu'awadah for the claimant, with distinct fiqh rules on spot exchange, shuf'ah, and moral vs. legal absolution.
